speaker_heading: ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS
dialog: India produces about 800 films every year but not even eight get documented.
text: There is no reading material available for film students or film lovers on the behind-
dialog: the-scenes action, the vision of the filmmakers, and the processes involved in making
dialog: a film. Lage Raho Munnabhai: The Original Screenplay was published last year.
dialog: 3 Idiots: The Original Screenplay is our second attempt in this direction. This book
text: is not just the screenplay; it is also a peek into the minds of its makers.
dialog: I would like to thank Smriti Kiran who took the initiative to come up with this
text: book within 5 months of the release of the film. She has spent many long days
text: and long nights chasing the cast and crew, writing and rewriting, with a smile on
dialog: her face. Without her persistence, this book would not have happened.
dialog: I would also like to thank Ajay Mago and his team at Om Books International for
dialog: giving shape to our vision.
dialog: Talso wish to thank Maheep Dhillon for tirelessly redrafting the screenplay; Supriya
text: Kelkar for her eye for detail; Rohan Mapuskar for poring over the dialogues;
text: Dileep Desai, Amit Gulati, Mustafa Neemuchwala, Teja Pratap, Insia Lacewalla for
text: production support; Anupama Chopra for her invaluable insight.
dialog: And Vinod Chopra for clearing all obstacles as always.
dialog: — Rajkumar Hirani

scene_heading: 03_Introduction.indd 9 5/31/2010 1:47:14 AM
scene_heading: 03_Introduction.indd 10 6/31/2010 1:47:17 AM
dialog: Every story has a back story and so does mine.
dialog: My father, Suresh Hirani, was fourteen years old when he along with the family
text: moved to India during Partition. Initially, they took shelter in refugee camps in
dialog: Agra and later shifted to Ferozabad. He had lost everything so education was low
dialog: on the priority list. There were more pressing and immediate concerns like food,
dialog: clothes, and shelter to worry about. To make ends meet, my father started working
text: in a bangle factory where he painted patterns on bangles. Later, he began selling
text: ice-cream on the streets. Despite compelling and adverse circumstances, his thirst
dialog: for knowledge did not wane.
dialog: My father’s sister, who was married in Nagpur, got him a job there in a general
dialog: store as a store attendant. The city brought with it opportunity. My father enrolled
dialog: himself in night school. Work in the day and classes at night continued and he
text: somehow managed to graduate. Fortified with a degree and some savings, he was
dialog: itching to grow. So when a friend suggested that typewriters, which were new at
dialog: the time, were the future, my father decided to explore this avenue. He invested
dialog: his life’s savings, borrowed the rest of the money needed and opened a typewriting
dialog: institute which was referred to as a ‘Commerce Institute’ then. My father christened
dialog: it ‘Rajkumar Commerce Institute’. I was not even born then. So now you know
dialog: that I was named after a typewriting institute and not the other way round! Most
dialog: parents would name their enterprises after their children, but I’ve had the rare
dialog: honour of being named after my father’s enterprise. I’m proud of that fact.
dialog: My father’s business flourished. He got married and I was born soon after. The
dialog: plan was to get into engineering but I didn’t have enough marks in my higher
dialog: secondary exams (12th standard) to make the cut so I studied commerce. I had
dialog: also enrolled in this foundation course for chartered accountancy simultaneously,
dialog: the classes for which were held in the evenings. At the end of the foundation
scene_heading: 03_Introduction.indd 11 6/31/2010 1:47:17 AM
dialog: course, the exams for chartered accountancy were to be held. I despised these
dialog: classes from the very beginning. I had no interest in chartered accountancy and
dialog: these classes were a huge imposition, both in terms of time and the fact that I was
dialog: wasting this time on something I knew was not for me. But I had the baggage of
dialog: the engineering debacle on my head so abandoning this very viable option would
dialog: have appeared self-indulgent, foolish, and luxurious at the time. I was afraid I
dialog: 
dialog: 
dialog: Rajkumar Hirani with his father, Suresh Hirani. Photo Credit Himmat Singh Sekhwat, Tehelka
dialog: would hurt my parents and also somewhat scared to take this step that could harm
text: my ‘future’. I carried on with the charade.
dialog: Subconsciously, I was aware that there were some set presumptions about my future.
dialog: I was expected to either become a chartered accountant or take over my father’s
dialog: business. Both possibilities mortified me. Though at that point in time, I was not
dialog: thinking about what would make me happy. Like any normal person, I had
dialog: responsibilities and the usual concerns that range from job to future to career. As
dialog: 12
scene_heading: 03_Introduction.indd 12 6/31/2010 1:47:17 AM
dialog: the time for the foundation course’s exams drew close, I would often wake up in
text: the middle of the night in cold sweat.
text: Finally, the exams were upon me. I realised that there was no escaping the inevitable.
dialog: It was imperative to tell my father that I found a root canal procedure more exciting
dialog: than chartered accountancy. Even after twenty years, that evening is as clear in my
text: head as if it had happened just yesterday.
dialog: Tt was a regular evening in Nagpur but for me it was going to be the most significant
text: and decisive evening of my life. My father was standing alone in the living room.
text: I walked in and walked up to where he was standing, taking lead-laden steps. My
dialog: throat was dry with uncertainty and fear. It was one thing to tell your father that
dialog: you did not want to pursue what seemed like an excellent career choice but it was
dialog: another thing to not offer an alternative plan. The only thing I was clear about
dialog: was that chartered accountancy was not my cup of tea. My voice quivered with
dialog: apprehension as I told him that I did not want to take the exams because I did
dialog: not enjoy chartered accountancy at all. He looked at me and then in the most
dialog: nonchalant manner told me to not do it and join his office starting next morning.
text: It was as short and simple as that. I was ecstatic. The weight of the world had lifted
dialog: from my shoulders. I felt free. This happened during the period leading up to the
dialog: festival Makar Sankranti which is known for kite flying. I remember running up
text: to our terrace right after ‘the conversation’ and flying kites with this silly grin on
text: my face. Now, the kite flying seems almost like a symbol for the sense of freedom
dialog: and relief that had swept over me that day.
dialog: My commerce classes in college occupied only four hours of my time in the
dialog: morning (7 am to 11.00 am), so the rest of my time was devoted to helping my
text: father with his business (11.30 am to 6.30 pm) which had graduated from typing
text: courses to repairing and selling new kinds of calculators and other office equipment.
text: I immersed myself in it with the gusto of a man who had just been extracted from
dialog: the gallows. I used to teach typing, go out and give demos to prospective clients,
dialog: and repair spoiled units. I was even sent to Delhi to do a course in repairing
dialog: electronic calculators.
dialog: This is the period where I had my first brush with theatre. I met Narendra Thakur
dialog: and a few other students who used to perform and produce plays regularly at our
dialog: college. I joined them. (Before this, my only experience on stage was in the 9th
dialog: standard. I was cast in the role of ‘Noorjahan’ in a play.) I became a regular with
dialog: the theatre gang. Abandoning the foundation course opened up my evenings to
dialog: 13
scene_heading: 03_Introductionindd 13, 6/31/2010 1:47:22 AM
text: be occupied, and occupied they were with writing, reading, and brainstorming
dialog: about plays. This stirred something within me. I have always believed that there
dialog: are two approaches to learning: one is to study something academically and the
dialog: other is to get interested in something and then start to gather material on it and
dialog: learn about it. The latter is always a more gratifying experience. I would hunt for
dialog: Hindi plays in quaint bookshops. I would devour anything on theatre. We would
dialog: participate in competitions, youth festivals, and travel to any place that even
dialog: hinted at an opportunity for exposure in the field. Youth festivals were a platform
dialog: where teams from all the colleges in Nagpur came and performed. At one such
dialog: festival, I met another theatre group which was performing for the Nagpur Medical
text: College. There were a few people in that group, especially Debashish Naha, who
dialog: were passionate about theatre and took this enterprise very seriously. There was
dialog: an instant connection with Debashish and soon our friendship led to my theatre
text: group merging with his to form a new theatre group called Awaaz. Debashish and
dialog: I would spend hours working on plays. He would get me plays written in Bangla
dialog: 
text: Finding his voice ... performing on stage (middle) for Awaaz, in Nagpur.
dialog: 14
scene_heading: 03_Introductionindd 14 6/31/2010 1:47:22 AM
dialog: and we would sit down and translate them in Hindi. We started putting up plays
dialog: under our banner. Personalities such as Marathi theatre-director Kishore Kulkarni,
dialog: who had received acclaim on stage were invited to direct plays for our group. We
dialog: learnt under many such people who had a command over their craft and a natural
dialog: flair for the medium. Since everyone was busy till 6 pm everyday, we used to meet
dialog: after that. These meetings were held either at the Bengali Association Hall or at the
dialog: Sindhi Gurudwara nearby which had a hall, or at the local school hall. Wherever
dialog: we found some empty space, we parked ourselves. The rehearsals used to go on
dialog: till midnight.
dialog: We pursued this with such passion that theatre soon became an obsession. We
dialog: managed to put up a record three to four plays a year. These plays were not restricted
dialog: to performing at colleges. We started putting up ticketed shows. Apart from the
dialog: creative aspect, the entire production of the play was handled by us. We organised
dialog: everything from booking the hall, putting up the sets, props, costumes, accounts,
dialog: and even ticketing. Watching plays was not the preferred pastime in Nagpur, so we
text: used to sell the tickets to friends and relatives. Accounts were maintained diligently.
dialog: In fact, I still have some of the accounts from those days. We used to barely cover
dialog: our production costs and that is all we wanted. We were not doing this for profit.
dialog: We just wanted to be able to put up plays and hoped to cover costs and use the
dialog: money to put up the next one. On those rare occasions, if anything was earned over
dialog: and above the production cost, it ensured a grand meal for our entire group.
dialog: It was not difficult to speak to my father the second time. Intuitive and observant,
dialog: he was not blind to my growing passion for theatre. The suggestion to tread this
dialog: path in earnest came from my father. He told me to enrol in a program to get
dialog: formal education (my father is a great believer in learning) in the field of writing,
dialog: directing, and storytelling. The natural choice was to apply to the Film and Television
speaker_heading: Institute of India (FTI).
dialog: My first attempt (1983) to get into FTII in the direction course was unsuccessful. I
dialog: had very little knowledge of the exam and the enrolment procedures. I had travelled
dialog: to Mumbai to take the exam. There I learnt that the entrance exam for FTII was
dialog: exactly like bank exams, and anyone studying for it would clear it without blinking. I
dialog: also realised that seats for the direction course were few and almost everyone applied
dialog: for this course. I came back to Nagpur and enrolled myself in the Faculty of Law as
dialog: that seemed like a reasonable option if things did not work out. Alongside, I started
dialog: preparing for the FTII exam. The next year (1984), I applied again to FTII but
dialog: 15
scene_heading: 03_Introduction.indd 15 5/31/2010 1:47:30 AM
dialog: 
dialog: Learning the grammar of cinema ... at FIL.
dialog: this time for the editing and not the direction course. I got admission. The day I
dialog: received the telegram, informing me about my admission to FTII, was the happiest
dialog: day of my life. I was delirious with joy. I thought I had arrived and my life was
dialog: made. This thought stemmed from naiveté. Sitting in Nagpur, I had only heard of
dialog: the ten to fifteen names of really successful people who had graduated from FTII
dialog: so I assumed that getting admission meant I was set up for life. I looked at my
dialog: admission in the Institute as one looks at getting into law school or engineering.
dialog: Once you get in and work hard, the moment you graduate, you become a director.
dialog: All these illusions were shattered two days into my time at FTII. My enthusiasm
dialog: was replaced by an inferiority complex. I was from a small town in the midst of
dialog: students waxing eloquent about theories, films, and ideas that were Greek and
text: Latin to me. It took me time but I found my feet again and got my voice back.
dialog: It taught me a very important lesson: you cannot lose yourself just by perceiving
dialog: yourself to know less. If stereotypes have to be broken, then first they have to be
dialog: broken within you.
dialog: 16
scene_heading: 03_Introduction.indd 16 5/31/2010 1:47:30 AM
text: Thus, began the journey of disillusionment and enlightenment, unlearning and
dialog: learning, passion, failure, and fulfillment. After three years at FTII, I moved to
dialog: Mumbai (1987) and stayed with my friend and batchmate Sriram Raghavan
dialog: (filmmaker). My first job was as an editor on an advertisement directed by Bharat
dialog: Rangachari. After this, I was recommended by late editor Renu Saluja and I edited
dialog: a documentary. I was paid Rs 1500 for my first job and Rs 500 for the second one.
text: But things were slow to come by. After sometime, the situation became precarious.
text: Video had just exploded on the scene so we were hard pressed for assignments as
dialog: we did not know how to edit on video. I could not even turn to my father for
dialog: help because he was going through a financial slump. His business of typewriters
dialog: had taken a beating because of the arrival of computers. So both my father and I
dialog: were facing what you can term a ‘technological gap’.
text: The first year in Mumbai (1987), after passing out from the Institute, was a difficult
dialog: one. I came very close to giving up during that year. There were many dark nights
dialog: of the soul. I thought about going back to Nagpur. Maybe do something related
dialog: to cinema there. It was a matter of survival, and the strangest options came to my
dialog: mind. I entertained the idea of shooting marriage videos creatively and putting a
dialog: completely unique spin to them. Maybe do multiple camera set-ups and scale things
dialog: up considerably. Exports, which was a much talked about thing back then, was the
dialog: other option that came to mind. I went to Fort in Mumbai (there are many street
dialog: vendors of second-hand books in Fort) and picked up a book on exports. I was
text: desperately groping in the dark trying to hold on to something. Fortunately, FTII
text: revived its one-month video course that was promised to us. We rushed back to
dialog: the Institute for the course. After one month, we returned to Mumbai with the
dialog: knowledge of what would be our survival kit in the city: video editing.
dialog: I joined an editing studio called Ekta Studio (1988) as an in-house editor on a
dialog: monthly salary of Rs 1000. My six-month stint here gave me enough contacts and
dialog: clout in the market to get regular work. I told myself, rather theatrically, that it
dialog: mattered not if I didn’t make a lot of money: this life I have devoted to cinema. I
dialog: convinced myself with something as dramatic as that. Once you accept this, then
text: the dark nights disappear and all nights convert to being nights of battle: the battle
dialog: to become better, to excel, to strive towards quality.
dialog: During this phase of finding my feet in the business, Sanjay Leela Bhansali, my peer
dialog: from FTII, who was assisting Vinod, called me to edit the promos of 1942: A Love
dialog: Story (1992). But things did not go beyond that just yet. Meanwhile, from editing I
dialog: 17
scene_heading: 03_Introduction.indd 17 5/31/2010 1:47:34 AM
dialog: had moved up to directing advertising films and opened my own production house
dialog: called Canvas Films (1991).
dialog: In 1998, I was called in to edit the promos of Kareeb as well because Vinod had liked
dialog: my work on 1942: A Love Story. This was again a one-off stint. I continued doing
dialog: work in advertising. Canvas Films flourished and I became financially secure.
dialog: I had settled into a comfortable pattern till the morning I received a call from
dialog: Vinod (2000). He wanted me to edit Mission Kashmir. Not just the promos but the
dialog: entire film. Initially, I was reluctant as I did not want to leave a lucrative set-up.
dialog: But once I learnt of Renu’s condition (Renu, Vinod’s first wife and film editor, was
dialog: suffering from cancer and her condition was going from bad to worse with each
dialog: passing day), I could not say no. It was during the making of Mission Kashmir that
dialog: my latent desire to direct a feature film resurfaced. Watching cinema being made
text: with such passion and commitment by Vinod and his team rekindled the spirit in
dialog: me that I had in my theatre days.

dialog: Miles to go before I sleep ...
dialog: 18
scene_heading: 03_Introductionindd 18 5/31/2010 1:47:34 AM
dialog: I knew I was ready to make a film. And I began working on writing my first film.
dialog: How Munna Bhai came about is another long story but that I will keep for the
dialog: book on Munna Bhai M.B.B.S.
dialog: So why did I just spill my guts and share my life with you?
dialog: Simply because so much of 3 /diots is personal. 1 am a small town man from
dialog: Nagpur with no connections or background in films. I started working towards
dialog: something that made me happy and that is all there is to it. I worked with passion
dialog: and honesty, and even if success had not come my way, I would still have been
dialog: really happy as I got to chase my dreams.
dialog: It has been a long and wonderful ride, but the key here is, it has been a ride of my
dialog: choice. The desire to make 3 Idiots stemmed from this thought. I am not trying
dialog: to say that engineering or chartered accountancy or any other profession for that
dialog: matter is any less than wildlife photography or filmmaking, and so on. My belief is
dialog: simple. Choose your own path. Choose without fear. It is not a foolproof formula
dialog: for happiness or success but it is definitely one that is most likely to work.
dialog: — Rajkumar Hirani

dialog: His Holiness Guru Ranchhoddas had

text: THE MVIETHOD IN THE IVIADNESS
dialog: Rajkumar Hirani, Abhijat Joshi, & Vidhu Vinod Chopra Recollect
dialog: In the course of putting this book together, the many conversations I had with Rajkumar
dialog: Hirani revealed that in a life spanning over four decades filled with many challenging
text: and debilitating experiences, the one that is clearly etched in his memory as the
dialog: most oppressive of them all is the month preceding his 12th standard exams. “Every
dialog: night I would get a recurring dream that I was taking an exam and I did not know
dialog: the answer to even a single question. I would wake up in cold sweat,” he said. Raju
text: attributes this to the fear psychosis created around exams and grades in India. The
text: first few daunting months at FTII, a year without work and money in Mumbai,
dialog: myriad struggles to set up a career, the Herculean task of making movies were lesser
text: strains compared to the pressures of Higher Secondary exams in his life.
dialog: It was no surprise then that when he started hunting for a subject he felt deeply
text: about as the DNA for his first feature film, he decided on the story of three friends
text: in a medical college. The aim was to weave a tale that enthralled with anecdotes
text: from life in a hostel and make a comment both on the education system and the
dialog: medical practice in India. For Raju, life in a hostel signified freedom. The time he
dialog: spent doing theatre with his group Awaaz with the students from Nagpur Medical
text: College, hostel life at FTI, and the horrors of education engineered towards grades
dialog: were experiences that had left an indelible impression on him. Therefore, the first
text: draft of Munna Bhai M.B.B.S. had, instead of the lovable Bhai and his sidekick
text: Circuit, three friends living in a medical college. The script and the ideas kept
text: changing and evolving. At the end, the three friends had to wait and give way to
text: the duo that became the Indian equivalent of Batman and Robin: the incorrigible
text: and immensely endearing Munna and Circuit.
text: But the initial idea that he started with lay latent in Raju’s subconscious for the
dialog: next six years. Such must have been his desire to make this film one day and
dialog: so great the need for the world to have this idea translated on screen that the
text: universe conspired to make it happen. Vidya Balan and the Mumbai deluge of
text: 2005 played significant roles in pulling this blueprint out from the deep recesses
text: of Raju’s mind. It was May 2005 and Vidya had just finished shooting for the
text: first schedule of Lage Raho Munna Bhai. She and Raju had struck up an instant
text: rapport often recommending interesting films and books to each other. Vidya
dialog: happened to read Five Point Someone during this period. She knew Chetan so she
dialog: was aware that he was keen to get the book across to Raju as possible material
text: for a film. Vidya immediately lent her copy of the book to Raju saying that this
dialog: was an idea she felt had legs. But being in the middle of making a film, Raju
text: did not have time to breathe let alone read. The book gathered dust on his
dialog: bookshelf for sometime. And then in July, Mumbai faced the worst rain-induced
text: floods ever. Life for everyone in Mumbai came to a standstill. Raju’s lane was
text: completely flooded and he had no choice but to stay home for a couple of days.
text: That is when he picked up Five Point Someone and read it. The story of three
text: friends and their experiences in an engineering college took him by surprise and
text: brought to the fore his first draft of Munna Bhai M.B.B.S. He discussed it with
dialog: Vinod and Abhijat. The rights to adapt the book into a film were bought and
dialog: the rest as they say is history.
text: The one-liner (a scene-wise synopsis of the screenplay and its trajectory) of 3 Idiots
text: was ready in a record nine days and the first draft of the screenplay with dialogues was
text: ready in 18 days from the day Abhijat Joshi, Vidhu Vinod Chopra, and Rajkumar
dialog: Hirani started working on it (2006). After that, it took the three writers, two-and-
text: a-half years to arrive at a shooting script.
text: The evolution of the screenplay involved ponderous work on innumerable drafts,
text: multiple scenarios (some retained and many abandoned), copious amounts of green
text: tea, long walks, and several trips around the globe.
dialog: The journey was through a labyrinth of possibilities. [In some drafts Pia was Farhan’s
dialog: ex-girlfriend; Chatur and not Suhas was to marry Pia; Farhan was an unhappy
text: corporate honcho in New York; instead of wildlife photography, linguistics was
dialog: Farhan’s passion; Joy and Phunsukh Wangdu were added and deleted frequently.]
dialog: The key to the screenplay was to find the best way forward that enabled the narrative
dialog: to be consistently compelling, real, and entertaining.
dialog: These interviews with the writers were recorded between January and April 2010,
dialog: at various locations, in Mumbai.
dialog: 322
dialog: There is a method to the three of you working together. How does it work?
dialog: VC: We have been working together for many years and have immense mutual
dialog: respect and affection for each other. Abhijat and I go back to the Kareeb days
dialog: and with Raju my writing association started with Munna Bhai M.B.B.S. 1 don't
dialog: sit with them and write continuously. My biggest contribution and challenge is
dialog: to channelise their passion and energies in a direction. I am their rubbish radar.
dialog: They do the writing and come to me constantly with material at regular intervals.
dialog: I either change some things or add new things to it or reject it. My work here is
dialog: of a good reviewer.
dialog: RH: Abhijat and I write constantly while working on the screenplay and once we
dialog: have a ready draft, we start the sessions with Vinod. He is brilliant at catching
dialog: the problems, the chinks in the script, the flaws. It is fantastic to get this kind of
dialog: perspective on one’s work as one is going along. It keeps us on the right track.
dialog: AJ: The interesting thing about the three of us working together is the diversity
dialog: in our methods of working which eventually enhances the material we work on.
dialog: Vinod’s method of writing and Raju’s and my methods are completely different but
dialog: our goal is the same: a great story.
dialog: Raju and I operate by working on individual scenes and later binding them
dialog: together which brings the desired unpredictability to our work whereas Vinod
dialog: likes his character arcs and plot defined. Therefore, when we take the scenes to
dialog: Vinod, he lends the necessary prespective to them. The principle which has been
dialog: the guiding metaphor throughout Aristotle's writings has been that the action of a
text: narrative or drama must be presented as “a complete whole, with its several incidents
text: so closely connected that the transposal [shifting an incident/scene from one point in
dialog: the narrative to another] or withdrawal of any one of them will disjoin and dislocate
dialog: the whole.” This is the principle that Vinod follows. His focus is the whole. Every
text: scene in the film must carry the story forward. It must have a purpose that serves
dialog: this movement. You cannot narrate a scene to Vinod and tell him that the purpose
text: of the scene is to make people laugh or cry. He will immediately throw it out.
text: Scenes might be luminous on their own but they are useless if they do not light
dialog: up the whole narrative is Vinod’s belief. Raju and I often get carried away by the
text: individual scenes. In the second half of 3 diots, Vinod took out a few dramatic
dialog: scenes which in hindsight I feel would have weakened our film. He believes that
text: we cannot overload the audience because after a while, they stop caring. There is
dialog: Fatigue that sets in. You need to have that balance where you don’t give in to the
speaker_heading: 323
dialog: temptation of overdoing things. To give you an example, the speech scene was
dialog: kept not because it was funny but because it had a purpose beyond making the
text: audience laugh. The speech demonstrated the pitfalls of rote learning and brought
text: the conflicting philosophies in the film, of chasing excellence and blindly chasing
text: success, out in the open.
dialog: This is the way in which we work together. Different perspectives enable us to
dialog: retain quality without losing focus.
text: The germ of the idea came from Chetan Bhagat’s book Five Point Someone.
dialog: What is it that drew you to the book?
dialog: RH: I loved the description of hostel life in the book. It instantly held my attention.
dialog: I had lived in a the hostel, like the boys in the book, so it was very appealing to
dialog: me. Education and the pressures one faces with regard to education that would result
dialog: in a ‘better life’, the one defined by society and not necessarily by the individual,
dialog: have always been of great interest to me. This gave me a wonderful opportunity to
text: combine these thoughts with a few anecdotes from the book.
dialog: It took over two-and-a-half years for the screenplay to come together. What
dialog: were the challenges that you faced while writing it?
text: RH: Five Point Someone, though immensely enjoyable, was very anecdotal in its
dialog: treatment. I knew the moment I read the book that we would have to spend
text: considerable time adapting it into a screenplay. There is nothing in the book that
text: binds it together. For a film, a binding thread is critical. The audience has to be
text: taken on a journey through the characters and plot. It has to move towards something
text: to hold the attention of the viewers. For example, in Lagaan, one of the things
dialog: that keeps you hooked till the end is the cricket match. The whole film is moving
dialog: towards that. I could not find this thread within the book and from there stemmed
dialog: the desire to create an external story. The idea for the external story came to me in
dialog: Kenya. The Kenyan Film Board had invited a select group of Indian film directors
text: and cinematographers to explore Kenya as a shooting destination for Indian films.
dialog: Therefore, Nikhil Advani, Shaad Ali, and I, including a few other directors and
dialog: cinematographers, travelled to Kenya. Initially, as usual, I was very reluctant to go.
dialog: What happens with me is that whenever I am invited to some place and if I am
dialog: working, I start thinking that it is a waste of time, and, therefore, why should I go.
dialog: This is the reason I decline most of the times and then I regret it. Twice over I passed
speaker_heading: 324
dialog: up the opportunity to go to Australia thinking that I am working on my script and
dialog: it would be a waste of time. In both cases, I could have easily gone and come back
dialog: and my life would not have changed. So when this invitation came, sometime in
dialog: March 2007, I decided I must go. I went but out of habit carried my laptop with
dialog: me hoping I would find time to get some work done there. On reaching Kenya,
dialog: I discovered that they had a packed itinerary planned for us for each day of our
dialog: stay. Obviously, they wanted to show us the maximum number of locations. The day
dialog: used to start at 6 am and end at 9 pm. After two days of intense sightseeing, I got
dialog: anxious about my script. I started getting very edgy. On the third day, I refused to
text: go. Nikhil and everyone else started laughing at me. I realised that if I told them
dialog: the truth, then I would not be able to escape so I lied and told them that I was
text: not well. They left without me. I took out my laptop and started working on my
text: script. Funnily, one lady who was with the film board, landed up in my room with
dialog: a doctor. I sheepishly told them that now I was better. Hopefully, when she reads
dialog: this she shall realise how pivotal this day of work in Kenya was to 3 Idiots.
dialog: That entire day, the one day I managed to work on my script, away from all phone
text: calls and distractions, I sat in my hotel room thinking about the story that I need
dialog: to introduce to bind the film. That is when this thought about the bet between the
text: rivals struck me. One friend goes away and then the other two search for him. For
dialog: the audience, the binding factor would then be — do they find this friend or not?
dialog: If they do find him, then what is the story of the friend? Why did he leave? Where
text: did he go? Does he win the bet or does he lose? At every stage, these questions
text: would be at the back of their minds. This desire and curiosity in the audience to
dialog: get to the end of the film is the backbone of a screenplay. If I were to remove
text: this entire story from the screenplay, then the film becomes just a story set in a
dialog: college and you are not really looking forward to anything. I remember I called
dialog: Abhijat from there and told him this idea and he loved it. The icing on the cake
dialog: was Abhijat coming up with the imposter idea.
dialog: AJ: The imposter idea in the film came from a real-life story. I knew a man
dialog: (I cannot name him as he is someone from the industry) who actually did what
text: the real Ranchoddas does in the film. A man applies to FTII and gets through. He
dialog: also applies to an engineering college and gets selected. He gets selected in many
text: places. He is a brilliant guy. Of all the entrance exams he clears, he decides to enrol
text: in the engineering college. This brilliant guy is our imposter’s friend. The imposter
text: wants to study cinema so he takes his friend’s permission and impersonates him. He
text: studies at FTII for three years posing as the brilliant guy who chose engineering.
dialog: 325
text: The imposter passes out of the Institute, joins the film industry, changes back to
text: his real identity and makes films. This real-life story, which not too many people
dialog: know about, inspired the whole Rancho-imposter track.
dialog: RH: We combined the search and the imposter tracks and got the story of the
text: film. The binding thread which was missing to keep the narrative moving was now
dialog: in place. This was probably one of the most critical of the challenges we faced in
dialog: writing 3 Idiots.
dialog: ‘What according to you is the core of 3 Idiots?
dialog: AJ: The first scene that we wrote for 3 Idiots was the conversation between Boman
text: and Aamir at Joy’s funeral. This scene was in a different form and shape the first
dialog: time we wrote it. On the night of the suicide, Rancho goes up to the tower in
text: the campus, rings the bell, gathers the students and delivers a speech. But in this
dialog: form, the scene became too dramatic, grim, and preachy. We worked for days to
dialog: make it more real and yet retain the drama and impact. The scene you see in the
dialog: film felt more natural so we changed the speech to a conversation at the funeral.
dialog: We purposely chose to make the boy who commits suicide a Christian so that the
text: funeral is set in a cemetery. The grey of the cemetery and the rain gave the scene
dialog: the grim and colourless tone that we needed. This scene, according to me, is the
dialog: core of 3 Idiots. Why should people be killing themselves? There is something wrong
dialog: with the system. Why is everyone running after grades and not knowledge? That
text: is what the film addresses and attacks.
dialog: VC: I agree with Abhijat. 3 /diots questions the system and its pitfalls. The pressure
dialog: to chase grades leads students to give in to pursuits that might not be the ones of
text: their choice and compels them to take the set and decided path to set and decided
dialog: goals. This only leads to suicides or mediocrity. Chasing excellence is not encouraged.
dialog: Excellence over success is a philosophy I have always followed which has led me to
dialog: take some very irrational decisions that have made no business sense. But because
dialog: the pursuit was for quality, in the long run, success came in leaps and bounds.
text: It is a very personal film. Not only does it embody a philosophy that is close
dialog: to your heart but it heavily borrows from your lives as well. Which portions
dialog: in the film have been picked from your lives?
dialog: RH: There is a lot in the film from the scenes to the quirks added to embellish
dialog: characters to dialogues that we have taken from our lives. I used to know a guy
speaker_heading: 326
text: called Prasan Jain in FTII who once had a bath at the entrance gate of the campus
dialog: to make the faculty aware that there was an acute water shortage in the hostel. So
text: Rancho bathing in the garden came from there. “Jahanpanah tussi great ho” is also
dialog: borrowed from the Film Institute. We were ragged for an entire month after we
dialog: joined. All throughout that month, if we spotted a senior, we had to pull down our
text: pants, bend over, pat our butts, and say, “Jahanpanah tussi great ho. Tohfa kubool karo.”
dialog: This practice was called Farshi. In my days at the Nagpur Medical College with my
dialog: theatre buddies, I got to know about this guy who used to slip magazines under
text: the doors of other students to distract them during exams. The competition there
dialog: was so fierce that people resorted to some really desperate things. The conversation
dialog: between Farhan and his father is again inspired by the conversation I had with my
dialog: father which I have mentioned in detail in the introduction to this book.
dialog: AJ: I have already told you about the impostor story. Apart from the search for the
text: third friend and the imposter track, the third primary element in the story is the
dialog: bet between Chatur and Rancho. This bet signifies the clash of rival ideologies. We
dialog: got the idea for this from an anecdote from Vinod’s initial years in the industry. After
text: the trial of one of the films made by a big Bollywood producer, Vinod hitched a
text: ride with him in his posh car to be dropped to the nearest bus stop. The producer
text: asked Vinod what he thought about the film. Vinod launched into shredding the
text: screenplay bit by bit and let the man know without mincing words that the film
dialog: was utter rubbish. The gentleman turned around and said, “Jadd vaddi gaadi lenga
dialog: na tadd dekhanga tu kaisi picture banayenga” (When you get used to riding in a big
dialog: car, then we will see the kind of films you make). The producer’s point was simple:
dialog: when you get used to the luxuries, it is what works at the box office that takes
text: precedence over the quality of cinema. Vinod got out of the car and swore that this
dialog: is something he would never adhere to. He was clear from the beginning that the
dialog: ball game was cinema and not money. If you got the former right, then the latter
dialog: would follow. This clash of philosophies, of thought, money versus good cinema
dialog: was the trigger for the bet between Rancho and Chatur. The Rancho “definition
dialog: of books and the machine” scene is my gift to Raju’s son Veer. Raju was helping
dialog: Veer with his homework. One of the questions in the assignment was: Why do we
dialog: celebrate Good Friday? Veer’s answer to that was “to commemorate the resurrection
dialog: of Christ.” Raju wanted to know if Veer knew the meaning of this answer. To his
text: surprise, Veer did not. Raju launched into explaining it to him and then asked Veer
dialog: to write the answer the way he had understood it. But Veer was adamant that it
dialog: had to be written only in the manner that he had written it or his teacher would
speaker_heading: 327
text: not be happy. Easy or uncomplicated language and, most importantly, veering away
dialog: from the expected and accepted form of the answer was not an option. Raju was
dialog: very baffled and concerned about this. He told me this story over the phone and
text: it became the genesis of the Rancho-Professor ‘definition-of-machine’ face-off.
text: The scene where Rancho, Farhan, and Raju go to Farhan’s house after the Director
text: sends the warning letter to both Raju and Farhan’s parents is also inspired from
dialog: two different incidents that happened in my life and Raju’s. Once, I had met a
text: lady who was furious with her daughter. The daughter was a bright girl but the lady
text: was yelling at her because she had scored 91 per cent instead of 94 per cent. The
dialog: 3 per cent drop was unacceptable. The insanity of this mad race for marks left me
dialog: amused and disturbed at the same time. When we were writing this scene, I really
dialog: wanted to incorporate this. While at FTII, Raju with his Institute friends used to
text: travel to Mumbai often over the weekends. On one such weekend, a friend of his
text: invited them to his house for dinner. They all landed up at his place eager to devour
dialog: the free food so looked forward to in those money-strapped college days. The friend
text: who invited them and his mother had had a big fight. They sat in silence waiting
text: for the announcement that the food is ready. After two hours, the mother got up
text: and told the boys, “Beta agli baar aaoge to khaana zaroor kha kar jaana.” Raju still
dialog: remembers this because it was close to midnight when they were asked to leave and
text: they were famished with barely over twenty rupees between all of them.
text: The watch track between Pia and Rancho again came from an anecdote that Vinod
dialog: had told me about ...
dialog: VC: See I told you I cannot remember half the things. Abhijat has been gifted with
dialog: a photographic memory. A relative of mine was working in the Gulf and was very
dialog: well off. I was not doing that well then. During one of his visits, he was flaunting
dialog: his watch which cost a couple of lakhs. I heard him out and then exclaimed that
text: there must be something wrong with his watch. He was taken aback and frantically
dialog: examined his expensive watch. There was nothing wrong with it according to him
text: so he asked me to explain myself. ‘Aapki ghadi itni mehngi hai aur meri sirf do sau
dialog: rupyaye ki lekin phir bhi time same dikhateen hein” (your watch is very expensive
dialog: and mine costs only Rs 200 but both of them tell the same time), was my reply
text: which left him speechless.
text: Rancho propels the story forward. He illustrates the philosophy of the film. A
dialog: lot of people felt that his character does not have an arc in the film. Rancho
speaker_heading: 328
text: enters the story, preaches, and then leaves. He is too perfect. How did Rancho as
dialog: a character evolve and did you have any fears and apprehensions that he might
text: appear to be more fiction than real as he began to take shape?
dialog: RH: The backbone of Rancho’s character which also separates him from everybody
text: else is his lack of fear. He is a brilliant, free-spirited, and honest boy who is
text: chasing what he is interested in most. Probably a person without fear has become
dialog: unfathomable today so Rancho might seem unreal to some but to me he is very
dialog: real. My first thoughts on Rancho were that he is a boy from a village who has had
dialog: no experience with formal education so he does not know the fear of teachers. He
text: speaks to them as he speaks to everyone. I could have dwelled on his childhood
dialog: and background a little more in the film to bring this to the fore but I eventually
dialog: felt that it was not necessary. Rancho lives by his philosophy and his beliefs. He
dialog: makes choices which are absurd conventionally but he is not here to conform and
text: he does not feel the need to.
dialog: VC: Why does he appear superhuman? It was very easy for Raju, Abhijat, and
dialog: me to flesh out Rancho as we understand him completely. In fact, I see a lot
dialog: of myself in Rancho. There are just too many similarities. I was always a topper
dialog: in my school. I remember being thrown out of class because I dared to ask my
dialog: teacher who Hamlet was. In college, I found BA very boring so I used to attend
dialog: MA classes and I even studied the books prescribed for MA Economics. In my
dialog: BA exams, I scored only 55 per cent. I was a consistent topper with scores usually
dialog: ranging between eighties and nineties. I was sure that there was a mistake in the way
dialog: I had been marked. I asked for a recheck and realised that the objections to some
dialog: of the answers stemmed from the fact that I did not follow the pattern of writing
dialog: the expected answers. I was quoting writers and theories, with all due respect, which
dialog: were out of the purview of my teacher. Therefore, when I took my final BA exams,
dialog: I wrote all my references with the page numbers and the titles of the books and
dialog: the authors. This is how I topped my BA exams. What surprised and baffled me
dialog: though was the resistance of the educators to reward inventiveness. This reluctance
dialog: and rigidity is steeped in our education system. You would be surprised to know
dialog: that FTII had failed me. I was thrown out without a diploma. Then my student
dialog: film, Murder At Monkey Hill, won the National Award and was also nominated
dialog: for an Oscar. The moment I won the National Award, FTII appointed me as a
dialog: member of their governing council. In this capacity, I spoke out against the staff
dialog: as I was aware of everything that goes on there. The staff protested against me
dialog: and said that I couldn't be on the governing council because I had failed. The
speaker_heading: 329
dialog: FTII management had to pass me then and I got my diploma. They had failed
dialog: me because they wanted me to take a lot of exams, some of which I thought were
dialog: completely ridiculous. For example, they wanted us to submit a feature film script
dialog: which I never did. The reason for that was simple. I felt we were not yet trained
dialog: to do this. I protested against these unfair practices. It gives me no pleasure to say
dialog: this but I did not have any teacher to look up to, and at that stage of your life,
dialog: a guiding force can be such a boon. These experiences and the lack of guidance
dialog: became my reason to back talent. I am passionate about doing this because I never
dialog: had that privilege. I learnt the English language at the age of 16 (as late as that)
dialog: and what am I doing now? I am making a film in Hollywood. Even now, I have a
dialog: problem understanding my actors and I ask them to talk slowly because it is tough
dialog: for me to understand their accents.
dialog: I can draw many parallels but the point I am making is that Rancho is not an
text: unbelievable, fictional, larger-than-life character. He is probably someone who is a
dialog: little tough to understand in this world because he does not conform. We are so
text: happy sitting back and accepting whatever is doled out that anyone who questions,
dialog: is considered an aberration.
dialog: AJ: While writing, we did not look at Rancho as a character in the film but as the
dialog: hero of the film and he has been written like that, though he is neither superhuman
dialog: nor a comic book hero. He has the attributes of a hero and it would be dangerous
text: to pretend otherwise. He is an uninhibited free spirit with insatiable curiosity and
text: genuine queries. He does not operate with arrogance or cockiness and Aamir has
dialog: also played him with that inherent earnestness. But it would be incorrect to say
text: that he does not have an arc in the film or a journey. His journey in the film starts
text: from Joy’s suicide. He would not have taken on the system had he not seen that a
dialog: perfectly wonderful boy was driven to suicide because of the mechanical, unthinking,
text: ruthless system education has been reduced to. This stirs something inside him that
text: he cannot ignore. A clear inspiration and influence here was the film Deewar (1975).
text: Amitabh Bachchan is a daily wage worker at the dockyard. All workers there have
text: to pay extortion money to Peter, the local goon. One man refuses to pay this and
text: he is killed by the goons. The death of this innocent person changes Amitabh.
dialog: He decides to fight back. “Raheem Chacha ab woh hoga jo 25 saal mein nahin hua.
dialog: Agle hafie se ek aur coolie inn darindon ko hafia nahin dene wala hai” (Something
dialog: that has not happened in 25 years will happen now. Next week one more worker
dialog: will not pay the extortion money to the goons), goes the famous dialogue. Without
text: this death which changes him, Amitabh would have remained just a worker content
dialog: 330
text: to earn his daily wages. This incident, its futility and cruelty, changes him. While
dialog: writing Rancho, Raju and I had discussed this scene from Deewar. Therefore, Joy's
text: suicide is a turning point in Rancho’s life. This is his journey. And in the course
text: of his own journey, he changes lives including his own.
dialog: Like Rancho, Virus’s character must have been a challenge. How did you
dialog: achieve balance there?
dialog: RH: During Munna Bhai M.B.B.S., we were shooting a scene where Boman was
text: to deliver a speech to the students. He improvised and added in the speech that a
dialog: doctor should be dispassionate towards his patients. He must maintain an emotional
dialog: distance to give him perspective. The exact line was: “Agar mein apni family par operate
dialog: karoonga to mere haath kampenge.” While scripting this, my debate with Boman was
text: that this argument is so logical and valid that it completely justifies Dr. Asthana’s
dialog: character. It makes my hero look weak. Boman looked at me and said that this
dialog: was my problem and not his. As an antagonist, he must have a point of view and
dialog: if that point of view weakens the protagonist, then the director has to make the
dialog: protagonist stronger. This has stayed with me since then. I have consciously applied
dialog: this while constructing the characters in my films. This helps in creating characters
text: that are real and not one dimensional. Therefore, Virus despite his quirks has a ring
text: of reality. His intention is not to go out there and harm students. He believes that
dialog: life is a race and if you don’t run fast enough, you would be left behind. This is
text: his point of view and he is unwavering in his belief.
text: The film starts with Farhan abandoning a flight that has already taken off. But it
dialog: was a long process before you arrived at this opening for the film. In the earlier
dialog: drafts I read, several approaches were tried including the one where you were even

text: large activity which Farhan has to abandon at the very mention of Rancho. This

dialog: AJ: Though we went along with the airplane scene to open the film, I do have an

text: of a conversation between two Heads of State at the UN. He is the interpreter. He
dialog: 331
text: leaves this session to meet Rancho and these two men are left in the lurch. There

dialog: to develop it so we went along with the airplane scene. The airplane scene was also

text: possibilities and then chooses the one that best works for the film.
dialog: Joy’s suicide was a critical point of debate. There are drafts that had it and
dialog: several that did not. Its inclusion was probably most fiercely debated in the
dialog: entire screenplay. It was felt that this was too dark an incident for this film or
text: that it might dilute the revelation of Virus’s son’s death being a suicide. What
dialog: was the thought behind the suicide scene and the reason for placing it within
dialog: 20 minutes of getting into the film?
dialog: VC: There was a lot of back and forth that happened over this. I had serious
dialog: doubts about whether we should include this or not. But what convinced me was
dialog: that this gave motivation to Rancho’s character. Raju took it a step further by
text: adding a montage and a voice-over at the editing stage. There is a small montage
text: with Farhan’s voice-over talking about Rancho and his quirky personality and from
text: there the voice-over says, “Ek aur tha bilkul Rancho jaisa.” And we cut to Joy. This
text: voice-over that Raju introduced at the editing table, integrated the Joy track into
text: the script. Otherwise, it would have been too much of a jump. It also illustrated
dialog: that a brilliant guy like Rancho can go one way or the other. He can either become
text: Phunsukh Wangdu or become so dejected by seeing mediocrity flourish around him
dialog: that he gives up on life.
text: RH: Joy’s suicide was thrown out and brought back innumerable times. Abhijat
dialog: and I were very keen to keep it as we felt that the conflict in the film would start
dialog: from there, otherwise it would just be a fun film without anything substantial in
dialog: it. Vinod and Aamir felt that it should not be there. They thought that it was
dialog: too dark and grim. It was a jarring inclusion in the film. But Abhijat and I were
dialog: convinced that this had to be there. This went on for a long time. In fact, we only
dialog: got it back into the script a month before the shoot started (September 2008).
text: But the debate over it continued and then finally the montage and the voice-over
text: during the edit, settled the matter. Earlier, the Rancho montage was not there in
dialog: the film. We had not even planned to keep it. Nevertheless, I shot it. Simply, so
dialog: that if I ever felt like reviving it, then it could be done. The feedback after the
dialog: first screening of the film we organised for a sample audience revealed that Rancho
dialog: being a mad genius was not coming across. I added the montage then and, linking
dialog: 332
text: it with Joy’s introduction by that voice-over, made Joy both integral to the plot
dialog: and also lent him credibility.
text: AJ: Joy’s suicide is the spine of the film. The second-half revelations are climatic
text: devices. But Joy’s suicide is what makes Rancho realise that pressure to perform
text: is the killer and it has to be fought. It is this belief that takes Rancho’s character
dialog: and the story forward. Otherwise, why is he doing the things that he is doing? The
dialog: first half of the film would become insubstantial if we took out the suicide.
dialog: The challenge was to put this thing that worries us about the system right on top
text: so that we know what Rancho is fighting, and the film is saved from sinking under

text: The interval point is a bit of a shock. The tone of the scene and the treatment
text: are unlike the rest of the film. There were other interval points discussed as
dialog: well before arriving at this one. How did this come about?
dialog: RH: I wanted the fact that Rancho is an imposter to be revealed earlier in the film.
dialog: I felt if we stretched revealing this till the interval, then it would seem that this
dialog: very rich kid had come into a college and was having fun. I felt that the audience
dialog: needed to be told as quickly in the film as possible that he was an imposter because
dialog: it would get the audience to feel for him. So in the earlier drafts, we came out of
text: the flashback one more time in the first half to reveal that Rancho was not actually
text: Rancho but an imposter. Then we went back into the flashback and there was a
text: different interval point (Farhan and Raju discovering that Pia is getting married
dialog: being one of the options considered). But after a lot of thought and work on the
dialog: script, I felt that the interval would be a great point to reveal that Rancho was not
dialog: Rancho but an imposter. Sometimes, I still feel that it might have been better to
dialog: reveal this earlier even though what we eventually put in the film is a great interval
dialog: point. In the end, these are choices that one makes. So once it was settled that this
dialog: was going to be the interval point, we narrated it to Vinod. He suggested that we
text: add the element of the college photograph having a different face on Rancho. This
dialog: appealed to me as it enhanced the scene.
dialog: VC: Unlike the West, Indian cinema does not follow the three-act structure which is
text: a two-act deal. It is a very unusual way of watching movies. No one else across the
text: world watches films like this — you see one part of the film, break for samosas and
dialog: chai, then you watch the rest of the film. This makes the interval point extremely
dialog: critical. Therefore, one must invest a lot of thought in where the break would
dialog: happen. In this film, my thought process was focussed on the level of interest of
dialog: 333
text: the audience when they would go and buy their popcorn. Basically, their eagerness
dialog: to come back to find out what happens next in the film is vital. When I made
dialog: the suggestion, I did not think that Raju would retain it. It lends a thriller-like
dialog: quality to the twist. I felt that the audience would enjoy the suspense. The first
dialog: time I told Raju, he said he liked it but I do not believe anything Raju says in the
text: evening. It is an age-old joke between us that I am the ‘in-the moment guy’ and
text: he is the ‘next morning guy’. He likes to mull over and think over all the options.
dialog: He makes up his mind only after that. So I always go with what Raju feels about
dialog: things the next morning and not on the evening that they are suggested. I am
dialog: happy that he retained it.
dialog: The film initially ended with these people finding Rancho teaching in Ladakh
text: in a school, leading a simple happy life doing what he loves doing. Phunsukh
dialog: ‘Wangdu was not in the film at all. How did the idea of Phunsukh Wangdu
dialog: fall into place?
dialog: RH: Yes, that is true. Phunsukh Wangdu was not in the film earlier. The film
text: ended with Farhan, Raju, and Pia reaching Ladakh and discovering that Rancho
dialog: teaches in this fascinating little school and that is his success. Whatever he wanted
dialog: to do, he did it. But we felt that maybe the audience would not understand this.
dialog: They would probably say that ‘yaar khush to hain, lekin paise to kam kamaata hai
dialog: na” (He might be happy but he earns very little money). We felt that to make the

dialog: way. This is the reason we added the Phunsukh Wangdu, coveted-scientist angle
text: and backtracked to plant him in the film. This is where Chatur’s quest emerged.
text: He was shown to be looking for someone in the entire film and at the end the
text: man he is searching for is Rancho. The idea was to show a man living a simple life
text: out of choice. Rancho has the option of signing a deal with anyone and raking in
dialog: millions but he has chosen to do differently. We felt this would help the audience
dialog: understand things better.
dialog: Another reason was that we kept feeling that the climax was not great. It seemed
dialog: that our climax was the delivery and then we were just wrapping up the film in
dialog: Ladakh. It felt weak. We, however, were doing a lot of things in Ladakh. First, we
text: were showing an unusual school. As Farhan, Raju, and Pia enter the school, they
text: see (an entire scene was there intially) that a race is going on, kids are running
text: and one kid falls down. Instead of carrying on, all the kids run back to pick
text: him up and then proceed to finish the rest of the race. These guys look at each
dialog: 334
text: other and say that this can only be Rancho’s school. Chatur’s electrocution scene
dialog: initially had Millimeter in it. So all this material was there but these pieces were
dialog: not coming together to result in a spectacular end to the film. When we found
dialog: Phunsukh Wangdu, things started falling into place. But to clean this up and to
dialog: get it all together at the end took us a lot of time. Finally, it evolved to become
text: a big twist at the end.
dialog: We solved the Phunsukh Wangdu riddle in our heads in Goa. Abhijat was on
dialog: a writing visit to Mumbai. I received a call to attend the Film Festival in Goa
dialog: (IFFI, end 2007). We decided to travel to Goa for two days on this pretext. Any
dialog: reason to get away from Mumbai and write. We stayed at the same hotel as the
