text: excited about it. He has this fascination for revisiting places that have a context.
dialog: Except for the chief minister’s party the night we had arrived, we did not attend
dialog: the festival at all. We took a long walk on the beach the day we arrived and
dialog: Phunsukh Wangdu fell into place. It was quite magical and we were very happy
text: with the climax after that.
text: Phunsukh Wangdu, the real identity of Rancho, is revealed at the end. The
dialog: point of revelation that Rancho is actually Phunsukh Wangdu is something
dialog: you battled with for over a year. What made you decide the final route?
dialog: RH: This was a tough one to decide. We were unsure of this right up till the day
dialog: before we shot the scene. Abhijat and I felt that it should be revealed the moment
dialog: the friends reunite, before Chatur’s arrival. By doing this, we would have revealed this
text: to the audience that Rancho was Phunsukh Wangdu, the man Chatur was chasing
text: and Chatur’s dialogues and demeanour with Rancho after this revelation would
text: become even more hilarious to the audience as they would know that he would be
text: in for a real shock when he found out that Rancho is Phunsukh. But both Aamir
dialog: and Madhavan were of the view that the revelation should happen at the end so
text: that the film ends on a high note. They felt that revealing it earlier would weaken
dialog: this dramatic twist. This is when the tussle started. Abhijat and I resisted for a
text: long time but Aamir and Madhavan were so vehement and insistent that it became
dialog: a question in our minds also. When I was in Ladakh for the shoot, I started to
dialog: feel that maybe their suggestion holds some weight. Maybe the surprise at the end
dialog: would be a great way to end the film. So I changed it and rewrote it in Ladakh a
dialog: day before the shoot. Frankly, I still don’t know which route was better. Actually,
text: it is a matter of interpretation. Both routes are correct in their own way.
dialog: 335
text: Chatur’s speech scene is a complete departure from the Raju Hirani sensibility
dialog: and the overall VCF sensibility. There were also varied opinions about the
dialog: shloka at the end of the speech. Were there any apprehensions at the time of
dialog: writing this?
dialog: RH: I was aware that what we were doing with this film, we had not done before.
dialog: There was also complete awareness of the fact that we might be panned for using
text: what people might label as toilet humour. There are ragging scenes with boys in
text: their underwear. The language is of a certain quality and other things as well,
dialog: but while writing, we realised that the campus world we had created would look
dialog: completely false if we didn’t use the language, the irreverence of youth, and the
dialog: whole college milieu while creating it. This is the way this world is and, therefore,
dialog: our job was to stay true to this world. This was the necessity of this world. This
dialog: scene we cracked when Abhijat and I were in Columbus, Ohio (April 2007). The
dialog: decision that Rancho will change Chatur’s speech was already in place but we had
dialog: not worked out the details of the scene yet. We decided to make it more challenging
dialog: for ourselves. It would have been easy to change the entire speech but if only a few
dialog: words were to be changed, then it would have been tough. Therefore, the challenge
dialog: was to write a speech that, if kept in its original format, sounded great but if you
dialog: were to change just a few words in it, then it would become completely different.
dialog: We struggled for many days to find the right words. We had thought about many
dialog: words and many ideas but nothing was good enough. We wanted it to rhyme too.
dialog: One day, we were out for a drive and we hit upon chamatkar’ and ‘balatkar’. But
dialog: after using this a few times, we felt that we needed a change because carrying an
text: entire speech only on a single word change might get monotonous so the hunt
dialog: for the second word started. I was in Ohio then, visiting Abhijat. We were out
dialog: for a walk when the second word struck us (dhan’ and ‘ttan). Abhijat’s daughter
dialog: Anoushka (age 12) was also with us. While Abhijat and I were talking, we cracked
text: this and Abhijat almost fell down laughing. We were both in splits but we could
dialog: not share this with Anoushka.
text: The shloka at the end is another story. It was not there in the scene. While writing
dialog: the screenplay, we had rented a small farmhouse in Pune where we used to go and
dialog: write very often. During one of our writing sojourns in this farmhouse, in some
dialog: other context and not in the context of this speech scene, Abhijat told me this
dialog: shloka. laughed to the point of tears. I decided there and then that I wanted it
text: in the speech scene and it was incorporated at the end of the scene. Since Chatur
dialog: already had this trait of farting and is called Silencer by everyone, the shloka fitted
dialog: 336
dialog: in perfectly. But Vinod felt we should take it out. Later, even Abhijat felt the same.
dialog: But I was very keen to retain it. During the edit, Vinod said that the scene was
dialog: too long and we should take out the shloka but Vir (Vinod’s elder brother) who
dialog: saw the scene was in splits and didn’t want me to touch it. I really wanted it to be
dialog: in the film so I was happy that eventually everyone came around to it.
dialog: VC: (Blushing) I was very shocked by that shloka. But Vir loved it and I tested it
dialog: through screenings on other people and they died laughing too. My objection had
dialog: nothing to do with sensibility because as Raju said, one has to adopt the sensibility
dialog: of the film and not some general sensibility. Therefore, there were no sensibility
dialog: issues here. I just felt that the scene was getting too long and might be a bit of a
dialog: stretch. My biggest concern is always that the audience should not be bored.
dialog: AJ: The speech scene was hilarious but I must say that it was not in the script just
text: as a set piece for humour. It is the illustration and the turning point for the clash
text: of the ideologies which is the key element in the film. Rancho protests against rote
dialog: learning that our education system encourages. He is completely against it. The
text: ‘definition of a machine’ scene introduces that. The speech scene illustrates the ills
text: of following blindly without understanding the meaning of things. It shows that
text: Chatur is end-oriented whereas Rancho worries about the journey. With the speech,
text: the battle of the philosophies becomes an open battle. Right after this scene, we
text: have the scene where Chatur and Rancho lay a bet to meet after ten years to see
text: who is more successful. The speech scene lent to the progression of this theme in
dialog: the film.
dialog: The shloka at the end is another story. I knew that if I told Raju this, he would
dialog: immediately want it in the film and that is what happened. Later in the edit, I too
dialog: was unsure about it like Vinod and like he said, it was not because of any sensibility
dialog: issues. It was because I felt that Rancho probably would not meddle with the
dialog: speech to this extent. He would change just two words and that would be enough
dialog: for him to drive the point home. But I am glad that Raju and Vir prevailed in the
text: end because the shloka is the pitce de résistance of the speech scene (laughing).
text: The delivery scene has invited a lot of mixed reactions from a section of the
dialog: audience. I remember you telling me that you began this film by first deciding
dialog: on the climax because that was a huge challenge you faced while writing Lage
dialog: Raho? Frankly, what were you people thinking when you decided on this as
dialog: the concluding episode in the film?
dialog: 337
text: RH: The origin of this scene is from Munna Bhai M.B.B.S.1 had thought of this
text: scene where Munna gets admission into medical college. He has only done this to
text: spite Asthana. He has no other motivation to study at a medical college other
text: than his disdain for Asthana. One night, he is roaming the streets with Circuit.
text: They are drunk. Munna is wearing the doctor's white jacket so that he feels like
text: a doctor. There is a Mumbai bandh’ that day. Both of them are crossing a chawl.
text: A boy comes running out of the chawl and is hunting for an auto-rickshaw. His
text: mother is pregnant and her waters just broke. He has to rush her to the hospital
text: or find a doctor. He spots Munna and drags Munna into the chaw/ because he
text: thinks that Munna is a doctor. These guys have no idea what to do but somehow
text: they get through the delivery. The idea behind the scene was to get Munna to
dialog: realise the value and responsibilities of doctors. The moment he held the newborn,
dialog: this would have hit him. Now, he would really want to study medicine. The
dialog: script underwent many changes and this route was abandoned. I had told Abhijat
dialog: about it. While writing 3 Idiots, like I told you earlier, we were determined to
dialog: first decide the climax. Abhijat and I talk a lot when we are writing so this is
dialog: one of the scenes I mentioned again and Abhijat said, “Why don’t we use this
dialog: here?” Rancho could apply engineering for the delivery. This thought combined
dialog: with a short story I had read somewhere about a man who used to talk to his
text: baby while he was still in his mother’s womb, connected with Pia’s sister and
dialog: ‘Aal izz well’, our climax, started taking shape. Then, we spent a lot of time with
dialog: gynaecologists to make it authentic. It became a big joke in office. Whenever
dialog: Vinod used to come to office and ask for us, he was told that we had gone to
dialog: the gynaecologist. Everyone teased us endlessly about our visits to gynaecologists.
dialog: One of my friends had introduced us to Dr. Sejal Desai who became our guide
dialog: while writing this scene. She told us about the various procedures by which a baby
dialog: could be delivered. The only procedure which could be combined with engineering
dialog: was the suction method. A lot of people think that this cannot happen but the
text: scene is totally authentic and it is possible to deliver a baby like this. At every
dialog: step, we consulted the doctors and, in a way, we wrote the scene with them. A
dialog: lot of work, a lot of research, and a lot of time were spent on this. Even during
text: the shoot, the doctors were there and constantly monitoring what was happening
dialog: and how it was being depicted. This is not one of those up in the air, fantasy
dialog: things that we have put in the film for the sake of a great climax.
dialog: You have often referred to the three scenes that constitute this continuous chunk
text: in the film as the soul of 3 Idiots: Raju’s job interview, Farhan’s conversation
speaker_heading: 338
text: with his father, and Rancho breaking down when they come and tell him
text: about their triumphs. These scenes had several rewrites before they took the
dialog: final form in which they are in the film. Why do you feel that this chunk is
dialog: the essence of 3 idiots?
dialog: RH: These are the moments in the film I am most attached to and they make me
dialog: very emotional. Even while narrating these scenes, I would often get choked with
dialog: emotion. In a way, I feel, they are the crux of the film. I have always been fond
dialog: of an underdog succeeding. I like hope. I see victory in hope. Be happy, go and
dialog: do whatever you want without fear. This is what one wants to say about life. We
dialog: had to put a lot of work into these scenes. For example, a lot of back and forth
dialog: happened in order to elevate the interview scene to the level we aimed to take it
text: to. It was written and rewritten several times. Initially, Raju Rastogi was only saying
dialog: things about fear, god, and superstition and he used to get the job. I felt that the
text: scene was flat. It lacked punch and impact. Then we hit upon the ‘attitude’ angle
dialog: which transformed the scene completely.
dialog: How this happened is quiet remarkable as well. I had read a chicken soup story
text: about a small boy who is asked by his teacher to write an essay about what he wants
text: to become. The boy writes his essay describing his dream, of owning a ranch one
text: day with thousands of horses. The teacher is not impressed and tells him to alter
text: this unrealistic aim to a more practical one. He tells him to aspire for something
dialog: more achievable as the boy comes from too poor a background to ever achieve this
text: dream. He also threatens to fail him in the assignment if it is not altered. The boy
text: comes the next day and again submits the same assignment. The teacher is furious
text: now. He tells him that he will surely get an F on this assignment. The boy says,
dialog: “You can keep your F Sir and I will keep my dream.” I was very moved by this
dialog: story. I narrated it to Abhijat and was itching to use it somewhere. For a year, the
dialog: subconscious thought was to apply this somewhere in the script but we could never
text: find the right moment or place for it. In the end, in a different form, it was used in
dialog: this scene. I think the thought here is of preserving what defines you. Once you are
dialog: willing to give that up, then that is the first step towards losing your identity and
dialog: your dreams. Farhan’s confrontation with his father, apart from writing it, was also
dialog: not an easy scene to execute. Even in the writing of it, initially, I was not entirely
dialog: satisfied with the trigger that makes the father come around. I felt maybe Farhan
dialog: has to say more, do more. Something here was not working for me. Also, I was
text: very unsure about executing the father’s change of heart. The tone, the blocking, the
dialog: pitch, everything had to be perfect for this scene to work. I expressed all of this to
speaker_heading: 339
dialog: Abhijat. I was very restless about this. He reassured me and said that he would do
dialog: something. He wrote this entire scene on his own and mailed it to me.
dialog: What clicked was this gesture he suggested for Farhan. The father gets very angry
text: during their conversation and comes and sits down on a chair. Farhan goes up to
text: his father and kneels down before him and talks to him. It was this act of deference
dialog: and total submission that elevated the scene. If you noticed, the tone of the scene
text: is not of rebellion but of persuasion. Farhan is asking for permission; he is not
text: informing his parents. He is appealing to them to let him live his dreams. If they
dialog: would not have come around, he would have sacrificed his dream.
text: Lastly, the scene where Rancho breaks down was not there in the film earlier. If
dialog: you look at it, there was no need for it technically because after these two scenes
dialog: (Raju’s job interview and Farhan’s conversation with his father), we could have
text: gone straight to the barber shaving off Virus’s moustache as he had lost the bet.
dialog: But I have always felt that when there is an emotion this potent, it has to play
dialog: out in full. You need to see the aftermath, otherwise it appears abrupt. The trick
text: to pacing a film is as much in speeding it up as in slowing it down and letting it
dialog: breathe when the screenplay demands it. I wrote this scene to bring the emotion
text: in the earlier scenes to its rightful conclusion.
dialog: But when we shot the scene that in its original form had Raju and Farhan come
dialog: and hug Rancho and he breaks down, I was not satisfied. I called Abhijat and asked
dialog: him about what he thought of them taking off their pants and saying, “Jahanpanah,
dialog: tussi great ho,” to Rancho. Earlier this was to happen right at the end when they
dialog: reunite in Ladakh. Abhijat liked the idea and we changed it on location. We
text: removed it from the climax of the film and brought it up to this point. Instead of
text: these boys, now Chatur takes off his pants at the end and says, “Jahanpanah, tussi
dialog: great ho. Tohfa kabool karo.”
text: There seems to be a design to the way 3 Idiots has been written. The drama
text: is carefully balanced with laughter. The audience does not cave in under the
dialog: weight of any scene. Was this intentional?
dialog: RH: This is an inherent trait in all my films. If I had my way, I would package
dialog: everything with laughter. I believe in the world that I create in my films. One
dialog: can choose to be cynical or one can choose to look at things differently. If you
dialog: really analyse the emotional, dramatic scenes, you will notice that they incite tears
dialog: of happiness and hope and not of despair and loss except for Joy's suicide. I hate
dialog: 340
dialog: sadness in films. So more than design, this happens because of the way we look at
dialog: the world: our beliefs, experiences, and ideas.
dialog: AJ: I agree with Raju but it is still gratifying to see that after every dramatic scene,
dialog: we managed to diffuse the tension by making people laugh within minutes. In terms
text: of craft, this is a massive challenge. It can ruin a film if not done seamlessly and
dialog: effortlessly. Our inspiration for this was The Lion King. Mufasa’s death is this dark,
text: hard-hitting, unforgettable, and memorable moment but exactly two minutes later
dialog: you are transported to the world of ‘Hakuna Matata’. We shed tears with Simba,
text: we are moved and stirred but two minutes later we are singing “Hakuna Matata’
text: with joy and hope. Most screenplays would dwell on an event like Mufasa’s death
dialog: for at least ten minutes.
dialog: Your (Raju and Abhijat) collaboration began during Lage Raho Munna Bhai.
dialog: It continued through 3 Idiots and now you are writing another film together.
dialog: It is a delicate relationship where many things have to come together for it to
dialog: become productive. How does it work for both of you?
dialog: AJ: Raju and I share the same sensibility. Our approaches, our ideas, the path
dialog: that we want to take, are all the same. When Raju and I write, the path that we
dialog: follow is completely non-linear. We start with writing the scenes that we value. It
text: is a nightmare later to string them together and beat them into a narrative. But
dialog: the magic of this method is that once you do this, you have, as writers, followed
text: such an unknown path that the audience has no chance of second guessing the
dialog: trajectory of the film. So we first follow the magic and pen down what we treasure
dialog: most and then apply reason and bind it together. It is a rare and wonderful meeting
dialog: of minds.
dialog: RH: I spend more time with him than I do with my wife. I wish he were a woman
dialog: (laughs). Apart from him being a good writer and our sensibilities being the same
dialog: (we are both from small towns and we have shared similar experiences; our reference
dialog: points are the same), the quality I most enjoy and admire in Abhijat is that he does
text: not defend his scenes. Most writers spend a huge chunk of their time defending
dialog: their scenes. If I want something done differently, he understands my point of
dialog: view. This does not mean that he is not passionate about what he writes or does
text: not believe in his scene but it simply means that he is ready to explore. He has a
dialog: positive aura. Secondly, I don't have to walk on eggshells with him. That is a huge
dialog: relief. In cinema, you waste a lot of time, precious time, dealing with egos because
speaker_heading: 341
dialog: everyone you are dealing with is an artist. You have to think of ways and means to
dialog: convey your opinion without hurting people or offending them. This house-keeping
text: I don’t have to do with him. I can say what I feel without hesitation and without
dialog: any sugar-coating. It saves us a tremendous amount of time. And most importantly,
dialog: Abhijat does not give up. We could have been through a million drafts but he
dialog: does not get fatigued by the evolving screenplay. He keeps at it. I am the same so
dialog: I know that this stems from tremendous passion. Even if the shoot is on and things
dialog: need to be changed, he has that faith in me and I in him that if it makes the film
dialog: better, we will do whatever it takes. This synergy is rare. Before Abhijat and I started
dialog: working together, I never thought that I would need a writing partner or I could
dialog: work with a writing partner but now I feel it is essential to have one.
text: The process of screenwriting and directing holds such mystery that many find it
dialog: hard to figure out where to start. What would your suggestions be to students
dialog: aspiring to write and direct their material?
dialog: RH: I believe that formal education in some form is essential to an extent. One
dialog: does not have to enrol in an institute or course necessarily but other modes of
dialog: learning like reading, watching theatre and films can help you gather the tools and
dialog: grammar that will enable you to write and make films. But what is most critical to
dialog: preserve is the faculty to think from your heart. Once you get into theory which is
dialog: important, we tend to lose the innocence with which films were watched earlier. We
text: tend to view them only with an eye on the craft: there comes the central conflict;
text: those are the hurdles in the plot; lensing just changed; and a crane was used for
dialog: this shot, and so on, is what we start focussing on. We start viewing films purely
dialog: from the perspective of craft. The ability to think from the heart and appreciate a
dialog: film honestly vanishes. For me, to keep this faculty to think from the heart alive
dialog: is the biggest battle. This is what leads to honest work. The other thing is to try
dialog: to bring in fresh ideas. It is very tough to do that these days but the effort should
dialog: be there to achieve this.
dialog: AJ: I feel that novelty of idea is paramount. Anything you are writing or attempting
dialog: to write should not have the sound of something that has been done a hundred
dialog: times before. The approach that though it has been done before but I will write it
dialog: so well that it will work, according to me, does not work. You need to feel strongly
dialog: about the idea that you choose and you need to believe in it. It must sound unique
dialog: to you to begin with. Once you have settled on the idea, the element of time comes
dialog: in. A screenplay demands a lot of time and patience. The writing process, in case
dialog: 342
dialog: quality is the goal, cannot be rushed. Therefore, you must be prepared to devote
dialog: that time. At least two to three years. Once these two commitments are made, then
dialog: for the first two or three months you must not write. You must mull over the idea.
dialog: Where do I want to go with this thought? What am I trying to say? Who are the
dialog: characters in my story? Why is what I am saying worth saying? Why should I be
dialog: writing this at all? These are the questions you should spend time answering in
dialog: these initial months. Once you have convincingly answered these for yourself, you
dialog: should start the writing process. A good technique is to keep two screenplays going
dialog: at the same time. It helps sometimes. You might love some moments but they don’t
dialog: work for a screenplay so you can always put them in the other one you are working
dialog: on or save them for some other story in the future. So you don’t hang on to your
text: darlings. But more than two screenplays at a time is a distraction.
dialog: VC: The only process and method I know is consistent, relentless, passionate, and
dialog: focussed hard work. Let me tell you a secret. Talent, as is the popular belief, is not
dialog: inborn. It is something you have to work for. If you work hard enough, you can
dialog: achieve anything. That is the key to it all.
dialog: — Smriti Kiran
speaker_heading: 343
text: THINGS OF BEAUTY
dialog: A thing of beauty is a joy forever ...
dialog: — John Keats
dialog: Praise this world to the Angel ... show him
dialog: some simple thing, refashioned by age after age,
text: till it lives in our hands and eyes as a part of ourselves.
dialog: — Rainer Maria Rilke
text: Three minutes of cinema time ...
text: A young lady who hates her father’s archenemy and furiously confronts him must
text: within the next three minutes break off her engagement with her fiancé, fall
text: hopelessly in love with the same archenemy, and convincingly fantasise singing a
text: rain-drenched love song with him.
text: The girl is Pia; the nemesis she must confront is Rancho; the fiancé she must plunk
text: is Suhas, the Price Tag; and the song she must sing is “Zoobi Doobi’.
dialog: The question is: How do we achieve this sea change within three minutes of cinema
dialog: time?
text: Raju and I ponder over this question in New York City over two cups of tea. We
dialog: know with absolute certainty that we can’t spend a single extra minute garnishing
dialog: the love story. Our screenplay, completed except for this one problem, is already
dialog: half-an-hour longer than we would ideally want. And the placement of the love
text: song is particularly precarious: around the 75th minute into the film, a time when
text: most people are itching to leave either for the restroom or for some popcorn. If the
text: screenplay sags even for a second before the song, audiences the world over would
text: end up using the song as a restroom break. And if they did so, they wouldn't return
text: from such a mass exodus in time for the interval point, the biggest twist and the
text: most crucial plot-point of the story.
dialog: We can't let them go for a break. Which means Pia must fall in love with her
dialog: archenemy in no time at all. But how?
text: In Shakespeare’s Richard III, Princess Anne goes to slay her husband’s murderer
text: Richard, only to get engaged to him in the same scene, mesmerised by his
dialog: allure. Richard later boasts: “Was ever a woman in this humor wooed? Was ever a
text: woman in this humor won?” remind Raju of the scene. Raju wryly reminds me that
dialog: we are not Shakespeare. Point taken. So how do lesser mortals achieve this effect?
text: Through some mundane, everyday thing. Some simple object, refashioned age after
dialog: age. In this case, it’s going to be Pia’s watch.
text: In their first meeting, Rancho had seen Pia being reprimanded by her brand-obsessed
dialog: fiancé for wearing a cheap watch at her sister’s wedding. When we wrote that scene,
dialog: we had not seen the potential of the watch as a /eitmotif. Now, in New York City,
dialog: where I have followed Raju from Columbus on the last leg of his US trip in the
dialog: hope that we can crack the Pia-Rancho track before his departure to India, we
text: decide to play with the watch and see what happens.
text: What happens is a little miracle that a screenwriter encounters once in about three
dialog: or four years.
text: Opening salvo with the watch: it helps Rancho deflate Pia’s fiancé with one swift
text: ‘demo: Rancho hides the expensive watch that the fiancé has just bought for Pia
text: and tells him that she has lost it. This simple ruse brings rushing to the fore all the
text: maniacal materialism, brand-obsession, price-consciousness, and the sheer rudeness of
text: the fiancé. Moments later, stunned and insulted, Pia breaks off the engagement.
text: Salvo two: Rancho has vanquished Pia’s fiancé but not yet won her over. It happens
text: in an instant, through a single innocuous remark. He congratulates Pia for showing
text: the courage in breaking off with her fiancé and points out that ‘Ab tum jab chaho
dialog: apni maa ki ghadi pehan sakti ho” (Now you are free to wear your mom's watch).
text: This single sentence causes an explosion in Pia’s cognitive universe. In a flash, it
text: alters her perception, memory, reasoning, and finally her judgment. How does Rancho
text: know that the cheap watch she was wearing at her sister’s wedding once belonged to her
dialog: mother? Because he could reason that the bride's sister, otherwise dressed opulently,
speaker_heading: 348
dialog: could be wearing one object so humble only for its sentimental value. Ergo he
dialog: possesses sensitivity and tenderness that she had never suspected he did!
text: Salvo three: The next time Pia meets Rancho, she holds out her wrist — she is
text: wearing her mother’s watch. Without a single word, through the semiotics of that
text: one gesture, she conveys to him that she has forgiven him his ruthless deconstruction
dialog: and debunking of her father’s and her fiancé’s philosophies, embraced his values and
text: is now firmly in his camp. In fact, she loves him.
text: Thus, the watch helps us tide over one of the trickiest plot points that had threatened
text: to derail our screenplay. Even more importantly, it infuses Rancho’s character with
text: one quality that sets him apart from almost all characters before him in Indian
text: cinema — authenticity. When Pia’s fiancé is ranting about the astronomical price of
text: the lost limited edition watch, Rancho gently retorts, “Meri to dhai sau ki hai yaar,
dialog: lekin time wohi batati hai” (Mine is inexpensive, but shows the same time). [This
text: is an incident that we borrowed almost verbatim from Vidhu Vinod Chopra’ life,
dialog: when as a young and struggling student, he could not ignore the slight about his
text: humble watch from an affluent relative.) This remark by Rancho is emblematic of
text: his authentic nature and the very first overt declaration of one of his many priorities:
dialog: value over price, passion over pragmatism, excellence over success, understanding
dialog: over information, communication over jargon, education over training, knowledge
text: over diploma, and a mother’s watch over a limited-edition watch!
dialog: All we asked of the watch was to save us a few minutes. What it gifted us was the
text: kernel, the essence, the very nucleus of the film.
dialog: Strangely, we stumbled on the extraordinary potential of objects through a problem
dialog: that we faced very early in the screenplay, a problem that is every writer’s nightmare:
text: a big cliché.
text: The scene: The Director’s motivational speech to the freshmen on their first day
dialog: at college.
dialog: Audiences around the world have heard and witnessed such spiel a zillion times,
text: ranging from the great bombast in Patton and Full Metal Jacket to the lamest
text: offerings from the trashiest of campus flicks.
text: Tom Stoppard, the great English playwright, came to the rescue. Not in person,
text: but in spirit — boleto Chetana. Stoppard thrives on introducing bizarre objects into
text: his plays and later providing perfectly plausible explanations for them. One of his
speaker_heading: 349
text: plays begins with a man opening the door of his apartment with shaving foam on
text: his face, a bow and an arrow in one hand and a tortoise in the other to find a
text: policeman carrying a bouquet of flowers standing outside. Soon enough, Stoppard
text: gives us an entirely convincing reason for this outlandish spectacle. Inspired by
text: Stoppard’s virtuosity, the cuckoo’ nest, complete with the fake bird atop it, was born.
text: The Director carries this strange object in his hand as he strides down the imposing
text: corridors. The audience wonders about the logic of this peculiar object, till in his
text: speech, the Director links it with competition that abounds in nature: a hoel kills
text: off its competition by pushing the host bird’s eggs out of the nest. As the audience
dialog: gets involved in the story of the koe/ and ruthless competition, we have successfully
text: negated a big cliché. Later in the same scene, instead of ending the speech with
text: rhetoric or polemics, we take it to its climax with another object: the Director takes
text: out a gleaming astronaut’ pen, holds it up enticingly as the ultimate trophy for the
text: students. Through Rancho’s healthy disrespect for the pen, and his archrival Chatur’s
text: salivating regard for it, we were able to immediately and wordlessly set up the clash
dialog: between two conflicting philosophies: obsession with success versus excellence. Once
text: again, an inanimate object was helping us not merely to scale over a plot hurdle
text: but in intensifying the film as a comedy of ideas.
dialog: Since one speech was served so ably by two diverse objects, Raju and I intuitively
text: grasped that inanimate objects would play a seminal role in the story. In fact, as
dialog: I have lately grown fond of saying, they would form the DNA of the film.
dialog: For Lage Raho Munna Bhai, we had discovered about halfway through the scripting
dialog: process that the DNA of the film was ‘olta kar’ or do the opposite — the principal
dialog: of inversion. For constraints of space, I can only give one quick example of this.
dialog: 
text: ‘When Munna is asked how to deal with a boy who desecrated Gandhi's statue,
text: from behind him Bapu answers: “Give more stones to the lad, tell him to topple
dialog: all my statues.” This is clearly inverse to the answer that the audience is likely to
text: postulate in their heads and therefore highly intriguing because they cannot second
dialog: guess what will follow. Throughout Lage Raho Munna Bhai, whenever in trouble,
dialog: we had summoned this principal of inversion.
dialog: For Broken Horses, another screenplay I co-authored with Vinod, the DNA is memory;
text: the childhood memory of a mentally challenged character. Whenever faced with major
text: dilemmas, he summons not his faculty of reasoning but the faculty of reminiscence:
dialog: what his parents taught him when he was little. In every major dramatic scene, we
dialog: found ourselves relying utterly on memory for solutions.
dialog: 350
dialog: For 3 Idiots, which was being written at the same time as Broken Horses, we realised
text: that the DNA was objects. Objects marked the comic and the tragic, the dramatic
text: and the philosophical beats of the film with equal felicity. To chronicle the genesis
dialog: of each object that served us so valiantly for transfusing red corpuscles into our
dialog: scenes would fill a book longer than the screenplay. But here is the briefest account
dialog: of our extended encounter with things.
dialog: The Comic Objects
text: The empty gift envelopes, the mounds of food on the plates of three friends, and most
text: crucially, the mint chutney are irreplaceable for the comic flavour of the scene where
text: Rancho gatecrashes Pia’s sister’s wedding. Of these, the mint chutney proved to be
text: a powerful motif. Rancho first drops it on Suhas’s shoes to determine their price.
dialog: We went on to use it in three more scenes:
text: A. In the song ‘Zoobi Doobi’ where Pia imagines Rancho as a chef eulogising
text: the virtues of the “ever-useful mint chutney that, apart from being delicious,
text: also reveals a man’s character.”
text: B. The scene where a drunken Pia barges into Rancho’s room and he, scrupulously
text: small town, offers her dhokla with mint chutney to distract her from her
text: amorous advances. This leads to the memorable lines about the menacing
text: names of innocuous Gujarati food items like dhokla, fafda, thepla that sound
dialog: like missiles to Pia.
text: C. The scene of Pia’s wedding, when to stop the groom from heading for the
text: altar, Raju Rastogi irons his clothes with an iron drenched in mint chutney.
text: As promised, the chutney reveals the groom’s character instantly as Pia hears
text: him scream out the price of his ruined wedding dress.
dialog: Our most innovative comic object, however, is not the mint chutney. The pride of
dialog: that place must go to an object so humble, so self-effacing that most people would
text: not even remember it — the heart-rate monitor in the hospital where Raju Rastogi lay
text: in a coma after his suicidal ‘high jump’. This object served an extraordinary purpose.
text: It saved Reel No. 7 of the film from becoming so grave, heavy, and weighty that
dialog: it could have sunk the entire film. This, by far, is our favourite reel of the film. It
text: starts with the Director’s brutal act of compelling Rastogi to type the letter of his
text: own rustication — an act that drives Rastogi into attempting suicide and culminates,
text: after Rastogi’s recovery, in the twin movement of Farhan convincing his father that
speaker_heading: 351
text: he does not want to become an engineer and Rastogi successfully facing a crucial
text: job interview. This reel is clearly the heart of 3 Idiots. The problem was that scene
text: after overwhelmingly moving scene, this was also by far the grimmest, darkest, and
dialog: the longest (about 20 minutes) reel of the film. We desperately needed to bring
text: some levity, some joy to this beat of the story. Since the searing dramatic scenes
dialog: allowed no scope for comic maneuvering, we came up with what I consider to be
dialog: the most daring and audacious tricks of our screenwriting careers — we decided to
text: lace the musical interludes of the song ‘Jaane Nahi Denge Tujhe’ with comedy. But
dialog: hold on! Swanand Kirkere has written those stanzas so movingly and Sonu Nigam
dialog: has sung them with such unbridled emotionalism that how the hell can we bring
dialog: in comedy into the interludes?
text: Well, with the help of the little heart-rate monitor.
text: Knowing that Rastogi’s brain responds to voice despite the coma, Rancho begins to
text: tell outrageous and funny lies to him to try and revive him. Though the idea was
dialog: comic, we realised that it would fall flat since the lies would not elicit a response
text: from the coma-stricken Rastogi. This is where the heart-rate monitor stepped in.
dialog: Whenever Rastogi responds to anything that is said, his heart rate increases and
text: the graph on the monitor alters. The dance of this graph started drawing laughter
text: from the audience at every screening. The more outrageous the lie or the action,
text: the more vibrant the externalisation of Rastogi’s inner thoughts. When Rancho
text: finally utters the most outrageous lie that poor Farhan would marry Rastogi’s sister
text: without any dowry, the seismic dance of the white graph reaches a frenzied climax.
text: The next moment the coma-stricken patient's right hand moves. Life returns to his
text: limbs, hope returns to his friends’ hearts and we, the writers, about to lose our
dialog: comic licenses for non-use, get a fresh lease of life. Thank you, monitor!
dialog: Apart from these major objects, there were many minor ones that came fleetingly
text: into the film to help us find chuckles, guffaws, and occasionally, that big explosive
text: theatre-shaking laughter that makes the years of the screenwriter’s toil totally
text: worthwhile: the spoon at the end of the rod that Rancho improvises to electrocute
text: the urinating senior; the single grey hair that is kneaded into the flour as the friends
text: are eating rotis at the Rastogi house; Chatur’s hand-crafied biscuits from San Francisco
text: that he so jealously guards for Phunsukh Wangdu, the comic device that enabled
text: us to weave in the Wangdu track without drawing attention to it; the urn of ashes
text: that the friends so shockingly and irreverently waylay and through the threat of
text: immersing the ashes in the sewer, blackmail the fake Rancho into revealing the secret
dialog: 352
dialog: of the real Rancho being an imposter, thus enabling us to make a long exposition
text: dramatic and funny. Pia’s helmet helped us negate the sentimentality and cliché of
dialog: Rancho’s dream [“Pia, tum roz dulhan ke costume mein scooter pe baith kar mere
text: sapne mein aati ho. Ghoongat ki jagah apna helmet uthaati ho....” (Every night you
dialog: ride into my dreams on your scooter, dressed as a bride. Instead of the veil, you
dialog: lift your helmet...) ] and last but not least, the two keys to the Director's office that
text: are flung at Farhan, one by Rancho and the other by the Director, one from a dear
text: friend and the other from a dreaded foe, one stolen and the other legitimate but
text: both thrown at him with the same aim: to save a human life.
dialog: The Dramatic Objects
text: A chopper with a surveillance camera mounted atop, rises in the air and soars. It
text: seems to embody the joy and goodwill of the entire campus in its flight. With
text: the crowd beneath giving it rousing cheers, it continues upwards like Shelley's
text: Skylark — “Soaring doth thou sing, and singing ever soarest.” Rancho, remote in his
text: hand, guides this invention to the window of its originator, Joy Lobo, hoping to
text: catch his astonishment and euphoria on the mounted camera ... and Joy Lobo
text: hangs from the ceiling fan, a failure in his own eyes. His most brilliant success,
text: the flying camera, hovers outside his window, recoding the inescapable evidence of
dialog: his flight from this world.
text: This is the biggest turning point in the film. From this point on, Rancho, though
text: an imposter, living under an alias and hence keen on keeping a low profile, takes
text: off his gloves and takes on the system.
dialog: I am ashamed to admit that for one full year, for the first five or six drafts of
dialog: the screenplay, we had something as lame as sleeping pills as the means of Joy’s
dialog: demise. Peel off a few drafts of the screenplay, and what you stare at is pure
dialog: mediocrity!
text: A few rings, a wheelchair, a laptop, and a letter, these simple objects provided us
text: with the bulwark for the most important beat of the story in 3 Idiots — the twin
text: movement where Raju Rastogi, a changed man after his suicide attempt, fearlessly
text: faces a job interview and Farhan, unyielding but loving, wins his father’s blessings
text: for leaving engineering and embracing photography. Without the aid of objects,
text: the scenes would have needed so many lines of dialogue to convey the complex
dialog: emotions that they would have degenerated into sermons. Objects helped us slash
text: the dialogue and deepen the emotion.
speaker_heading: 353
text: Before facing the job interview, Raju Rastogi takes off his lucky rings that embody
text: all his superstitions and fears and discards them in a metallic bedpan. CLANG!
text: Wordlessly, with that mutinous clang, the hitherto weak man has trashed his old
text: fearful self. Later, the wheelchair is crucial to the scene when he faces the interview
text: panel. More than what he has lost, the wheelchair represents what he has gained:
text: “Dono taange tudvaake apne pairon pe khada rahena seekha hai” (It took two broken
text: legs to get me up on my feet). The same scene, without changing a single line
text: of dialogue, with Rastogi sitting in an ordinary chair would never have had the
dialog: same impact.
text: A laptop versus a letter — that is the essence of the encounter between Farhan
text: and his father. The letter is Farhan’s dream, written five years ago to his revered
dialog: photographer requesting apprenticeship but never posted for fear of breaking his
text: father’s heart. Rancho sends it out without Farhan’s knowledge and lo! the photographer
text: answers, inviting Farhan to the Brazilian rainforest to work with him. Now Farhan
text: stands at two divergent roads, one that leads him to engineering and the other to
dialog: his passion, photography. Which one will he take? At this very moment, Farhan’s
text: father is proudly showing his mother the brand new laptop, a gift for Farhan, in
text: anticipation of a plush new engineering job. The laptop represents the recompense
dialog: for all the sacrifices and hardships that the father has undertaken for the education
text: of his son and all his solid and conservative hopes of a good life for his son. In
dialog: this sense, the laptop is the exact opposite of the sense of adventure, uncertainty
text: and passion that the letter represents. The scene becomes the battleground for two
text: disparate philosophies through two commonplace objects. When Farhan, without
text: an ounce of anger or bitterness, with immense love and respect, makes his father
text: see the validity of his argument, the letter wins. The father tells the mother to
text: return the laptop and buy a professional camera for his son.
text: Another such scene where two rival philosophies clash appears earlier in the film —
text: the scene of the vital bet between Chatur and Rancho. It is a clash not of individuals
dialog: but of priorities and values: Chatur’s obsession with success versus Rancho’s respect
text: for excellence. The rivals decide on a date ten years later, when they will meet and
text: compare notes on who won and who lost. While there is no overt use of objects in
dialog: the scene, we achieved the ‘concretisation’ or the ‘solidification’ effect through getting
text: Chatur to carve out a date on the mossy wall of the terrace. By being engraved on
text: the wall, the date became tangible, solid, permanent. It received an import that it
text: lacked when it was merely uttered verbally in earlier drafts. Chatur carves the date
text: not merely on the wall but also on the psyche of the audience.
speaker_heading: 354
text: Perhaps the most telling example of this ‘solidification’ or ‘concretisation’ of a scene
dialog: is the interval point of the film. Both Raju and I were extremely proud of our twist:
text: afier a long and eventful journey, the friends seem to reach Rancho, but when Farhan
text: puts his hand on the man’ shoulder, the man who turns and looks at them is not their
text: friend. It is a complete stranger. The friends are shocked. INTERMISSION. When we
dialog: narrated this scene to Vinod, he didn’t share our enthusiasm. He was uneasy and
text: restless till he realised what he was missing in the scene. A tangible deepening of
text: the mystery. Vinod felt that without solid evidence of some kind, the audience
dialog: might take the encounter with the wrong man as some minor misunderstanding,
dialog: a communication gaffe that might soon be sorted out. So he added a few extra
text: seconds to our scene: Afier meeting the stranger, Farhan sees on the wall, a group photo
text: of the classmates, featuring all three friends but the place where Rancho sat in the real
text: photo is now occupied by this total stranger. The wrong man. INTERMISSION. The
text: inanimate photo provides the scene with the final turn of the screw. The nebulous
text: mystery suddenly becomes concrete. The plot thickens.
dialog: Am I reading too much into this concept of ‘solidification’ or ‘concretisation’
dialog: through objects? Am I zealously overanalysing a moot point? Are there no major
dialog: scenes in 3 Idiots that do not rely on objects? Wrong. In fact, the most famous
dialog: scene of the film, Chatur’s speech that goes outrageously wrong [chamatkar versus
dialog: balatkar, dhan versus sttun] relies entirely on wordplay. So does my favorite scene:
text: Rancho’s deliberately obscure, whimsical, and wooly-headed definition of books
dialog: [“Instruments that record, analyse, summarise, organise, debate and explain information;
text: that are illustrated, non-illustrated, hard-bound, paperback, jacketed, non-jacketed; with
dialog: foreword, introduction, table-of-contents, index; that are intended for the enlightenment,
dialog: understanding, enrichment, enhancement and education of the human brain through the
dialog: sensory route of vision. Sometimes touch.”] In both these scenes we relied exclusively
dialog: on verbal dexterity. In one instance crude, in the other, sophisticated. Do these
dialog: scenes, then, constitute the antithesis of my argument? Hardly. Because these scenes,
text: if anything, serve to demonstrate the devastating effects of the lack of ‘concrete’
dialog: understanding and ‘solid’ conceptual clarity that plague our educational system.
text: The wordplay scenes are meant, as Rancho would put it, to be a ‘demo’ of what
text: obfuscation, empty jargon, hyperbole, pomposity, rote learning bring upon us —
dialog: nothing but ‘balaktkar pe balatkar’.
dialog: Objects were our closest allies for three years. Like a friend that you can confidently
dialog: call up in the middle of the night at the time of a crisis, we knocked on the door of
dialog: objects every time we were in trouble. Every time, they responded generously. Most
speaker_heading: 355
dialog: times, like good friends, they didn’t make a big show of the help they had extended
text: and remained largely inconspicuous. Almost invisible. But every object I listed here
text: is now a part of what Rogers and Hammerstein have famously described as “a few
text: of my favorite things.” Right from the official stamp that seals Farhan’s fate at the
text: beginning of the film, to the astronauts pen that with one signature turns Chatur’s
text: gloating victory into a humiliating defeat at the very end of the film, 3 Idiots, could
dialog: not have been written without dreaming up hundreds of such objects. The most
dialog: menial of these objects, are for me, things of beauty. They took our hollow scenes
dialog: and filled them with humor or drama; they took our most abstract concepts and
dialog: made them accessible.
text: In Shakepeare’s words, they gave “the airy nothing a local habitation and a name.”
dialog: — Abhijat Joshi

speaker_heading: CASTING A SPELL
text: From the suppressed frustration of the hospital sweeper in Munna Bhai M.B.B.S,

dialog: to get his pension, to the sparkling purity of Chhote in 3 idiots, while it may be

dialog: Rajkumar Hirani believes that the face maketh the character. If anything comes as
text: a close second to the script in his school of filmmaking, it is casting. Every face
text: visible in a Rajkumar Hirani film has been hand-picked by him. “Casting is half
dialog: the job done as far as characters in the film are concerned. Sometimes, we find the
dialog: right people and at times, the right people find us,” says the man who drives his
dialog: ADs (assistant directors) and casting team literally up the wall even for the right
text: junior artists meant to provide the ‘background’ in a scene.
text: Tt took Raju a year to freeze on the actor for Joy Lobo’s father in 3 Idiots, a character
text: that appears for barely two minutes on screen. Over five hundred screen tests later,
dialog: he exclaimed with frustration, “Maybe honest faces are vanishing from this planet.”
dialog: The brief was to find a face that would be the epitome of selfless love, kindness,
text: and innocence. Every heart in the theatre would break when he mourned his son’s
text: untimely death. Raju’s associate, Rajesh Mapuskar, came to the rescue and suggested
dialog: child actor, Madhav Vaze, from the acclaimed Marathi film Shyamchi Ayee (Shyam’s
dialog: mother) made in 1953. He would be of the right age now to play the role. The
dialog: hunt for the actor began and he was finally located in Pune. The only question
dialog: was: had Madhav’s face retained the same stirring innocence it had fifty-six years
text: ago? One look at him and Raju knew he had found Mr. Lobo.
dialog: And sometimes, the right actor finds his/her way to the right role. Every project
text: goes through various stages of casting. A single change in the script could have
dialog: one consider someone else rather than the actor initially under consideration for a
dialog: particular role. If one were to imagine anyone other than Aamir Khan as Rancho,
text: Kareena Kapoor as Pia, or Sanjay Dutt as Munna Bhai, the entire perception and
dialog: tone of these characters would change in our heads.
text: The principal cast of 3 Idiots came together with all the serendipitous twists and

dialog: What do you think is the core of 3 Idiots?

text: Three weeks later he received a message from Raju Hirani.
text: The next day, Madhavan boarded a flight to Mumbai.
dialog: You were approached for Munna Bhai at the time Raju was thinking of making
dialog: it as a television series. What was the meeting like?
dialog: I don’t remember meeting Raju for the television series. I was approached and we
dialog: had met but I have no recollection of the meeting. It was only when Munna Bhai
dialog: M.B.B.S. released that I came to know that it was this particular film that Raju
dialog: had wanted to make for television for which he had approached me.
dialog: I remember seeing Munna Bhai M.B.B.S. and saying where had this guy been
dialog: hiding all this while. It is a sign of greatness to be able to say so many things in
dialog: the simplest manner. He struck me as a filmmaker and not merely as a director.
dialog: I desperately wanted to get in touch with him. I remember gushing and raving in
dialog: the message I sent him after watching the film. He immediately called me back
dialog: and told me that he likes my work immensely.
dialog: ‘We met sporadically after that at award functions and social gatherings. I had even
dialog: approached Raju to write the screenplay of Ramji Londonwale because I thought it
dialog: was a subject that might appeal to him. He was busy with Lage Raho at the time
dialog: but he was kind enough to suggest Abbas Tyrewala for it. Abbas was also caught
dialog: up with his own film. Eventually, Sanjay Dayma wrote the film. But I knew the
dialog: moment I met Raju that our connection was for keeps.
dialog: How did 3 Idiots happen? Your belief is that the process started with you
dialog: watching the film Secret. What happened when you got the phone call?
dialog: Sarita, my wife, had been telling me to get in touch with Raju. She had heard that
dialog: he was making a film that revolved around three engineering students. I reasoned
dialog: with her that films don’t get made that way. If Raju had not already considered me
dialog: for a role, it wouldn't be right for me and I did not want to put him in a spot
text: by calling him. When I got the message from him, Sarita and I sat staring at it in
dialog: stunned silence. I don’t know how much this has to do with Secret but this was such
speaker_heading: 378
dialog: an amazing coincidence. The message was simple: “Maddy, I need to speak with you.”
dialog: Even before calling him back, I told Sarita that I was doing a film with Raju.
dialog: Then I called up Raju and he politely asked me to meet him whenever I was in
dialog: Mumbai next. I told him, “I am coming tomorrow.” I remember going to his office
dialog: and meeting him. He narrated the script to me which made me laugh and cry at
dialog: the same time.
dialog: I immediately offered to give him a screen test. He was aghast but I insisted. I picked
text: out two scenes from the script. One of them was eventually not in the film. This
text: is the scene where one of the puppies I have grown fond of in the hostel dies and
dialog: I get drunk and react. The other scene was the scene with my father in the film
dialog: where I tell him that I don’t want to pursue engineering. Raju was not going to take
dialog: a decision right then even though I could see that he was convinced. He said that
dialog: he would let me know. I did not hear from him for two weeks. Then I received a
dialog: mail from him saying that Vinod Chopra would call me. This was somewhere in
dialog: April 2008 and we were supposed to shoot in August. After waiting for some more
dialog: time, I called Raju and asked him if I could give my dates away as I had not heard
dialog: from them and my other producers were waiting. Raju was shocked to hear this
dialog: and said that I absolutely couldn't give away my dates because we were shooting
dialog: in July. Apparently there was some confusion and I had not been informed. Then
dialog: Vinod called and things were finalised.
dialog: Did you audition for both the roles or was it always Farhan?
dialog: Farhan was the only role I related to when I heard the script. I think even Raju had
dialog: only Farhan in mind as far as I was concerned. When Raju narrated the script to me,
dialog: I knew that every student and every father in the country would relate to Farhan.
dialog: If I was able to pull this role off, then that would be my biggest achievement.
dialog: What were your first reactions to the script?
dialog: The best part about Raju is that he encourages us to think like the characters we
dialog: are to play. Our feedback is considered and taken very seriously. As an example,
dialog: for a long time, I felt that I did not have enough of a trigger to go confront my
dialog: father. Initially, the letter to Andre Istevan was not there in the script. I spoke to
dialog: Raju several times about this. Two or three weeks later, Raju told me about the
dialog: idea of the letter to Andre Istevan that Farhan never posted, and that became
dialog: the trigger. I did not even know that Raju had taken my apprehension seriously
speaker_heading: 379
dialog: and had been working on it. Raju came up with this idea and then he and Abhijat
dialog: planted the fact that I am an animal lover with a natural talent and interest in
dialog: wildlife photography.
dialog: The film started its principal photography with your scene in the airplane.
dialog: ‘What was your approach to Farhan as he is also the narrator of the film?
dialog: The first transformation you see on screen is in Farhan. So there was the additional
dialog: challenge of making that believable. If the audience did not find me convincing,
dialog: then it would set the wrong tone for the film. I set about working on my look first.
dialog: Also as a character, I go through maximum transformation. Raju’s transformation
text: happens in college after his suicide attempt. He loses his fears and there is a complete
text: change in his attitude evident in the interview scene but Farhan’s transformation
dialog: in terms of his personality was to happen once he left college. There had to be a
text: marked difference between the Farhan the audience would see in the first scene
dialog: and the one they would see in college. I gave myself a darker, leaner, and rugged
dialog: look for the portions in the present. I am a wildlife photographer and, therefore,
dialog: spend time in the sun, wear those jackets with many pockets to hold things that
text: come in handy on a shoot and name an edgier personality. There is an aggressive
text: dimension to Farhan, the wildlife photographer. But as an engineering student, he
text: is mild and innocent. I went completely clean shaven, chubby cheeked with round
dialog: boyish glasses. It took us a very long time to zero in on the glasses, I remember.
dialog: So, I worked on the look as well as the personality to make sure that the journey
text: of the character is evident and real.
scene_heading: Hf CHATUR RAMALINGAM: OMI VAIDYA
dialog: We took a huge gamble by casting Omi. He has a long and important role in the film
dialog: and we were casting someone for it who did not know Hindi at all. It was a huge
dialog: chance we took which paid off eventually. Omi is a great find. He brought credibility
dialog: and believability to Chatur’ character which was critical for it to work.
dialog: — Rajkumar Hirani
text: His feet were killing him. The entire day he had spent running from pillar to post
dialog: taking care of proceedings at a grand Marathi event that he had organised in Los
dialog: Angeles. A lot of people from the Marathi community in America had flown in to
text: attend the event. Finally, the night was winding down and it was time to let go
text: and have some fun. He hit the dance floor and inadvertently started dancing with
dialog: 380
text: a girl. She was a screenwriter from Michigan and he, a working actor and film
text: director. They exchanged numbers.
text: Four years later, the actor-director while on a visit to India to find possible buyers
text: for his film, got a call from her — out of the blue. Supriya Kelkar, the screenwriter
dialog: from Michigan was now working with Vinod Chopra on Broken Horses and wanted
text: to give Omi Vaidya a heads-up on a possible role in their production 3 Idiots.
dialog: Omi Vaidya might never have bagged this career-altering role were it not for that
text: random dance four years ago at a Marathi night in Los Angeles.
dialog: You have lived in America all your life and are a working actor in LA. Was
dialog: Hindi Cinema on your agenda?
dialog: Both my parents were very supportive of my choice to pursue acting, otherwise
dialog: it would have been very difficult to do this. Most Indians settled abroad might
dialog: not have managed to do what I did. I had my family backing me and that is
dialog: the reason it was possible. My mother had wanted to become an actress but she
dialog: could never pursue that desire so she made sure that at least I could follow my
dialog: dreams. Therefore, I did not have to worry about things like rent and food after
dialog: college and was able to concentrate only on acting. Bollywood, strangely, was never
dialog: on the radar nor was the sensibility preferred or familiar. I did not know much
dialog: about Bollywood films. I had seen some of Aamir Khan's films. But three-hour-
dialog: long films with all the dancing made little sense to me and the sensibility did not
dialog: hold much appeal for me either. I had seen a bit of Munna Bhai M.B.B.S. but
dialog: nothing of Lage Raho. All of this, I later realised, was actually a boon. It was an
dialog: asset to have little knowledge of Bollywood. It helped me when I was shooting
dialog: with the top-of-the-line actors like Aamir Khan in 3 Jdots. 1 was not weighed
dialog: down by the awe of shooting with the biggest star in Bollywood. I could perform
dialog: without being hampered.
dialog: How did 3 Idiots happen and what drew you to the material despite no prior
dialog: plans or inclination to be part of Hindi Cinema?
dialog: When I got a call from Supriya, I sent my showreel across and Raju liked it because
dialog: he felt that I was very boyish and, most importantly, I was not from India. Initially,
dialog: I was very sceptical. I informed them that I didn’t speak the language at all so
dialog: how would this be feasible. The people scheduled to audition me just refused to
dialog: listen assuming that I would at least have some knowledge of the language because
speaker_heading: 381
dialog: everybody who says they don’t know the language, do know a little bit. Little did
dialog: they know that I really did not know the language at all.
dialog: I was auditioned on Raju Rastogi’s lines from the film and I was terrible. I spent
text: some three hours in the office reading the material. During the screen test, 30 seconds
dialog: after I started saying the lines, the casting director started correcting me and a
dialog: minute into the audition, she asked me to stop. I was politely thanked so I knew
dialog: that they had not been impressed but then I had gone in knowing that I would
dialog: fail, What worked in my favour was that Raju came into the office while I was
dialog: meeting his team. He had some work there so I ran into him. We got to chat a
dialog: bit and therefore a personal connection was established.
dialog: A few days later, I received a call from the team asking me to come in again. And
dialog: this time, they said that they would send me a script for the audition. They sent me
dialog: a portion from Munna Bhai. The brief was that I would have to learn the lines and
dialog: not speak them. I was not to worry about speaking Hindi correctly: the character
dialog: was an NRI though he had not been fully fleshed out as yet but I was just to do
dialog: what Omi would do. I felt much more comfortable with this. The way I did it was
dialog: that even though I knew what I was saying, I did it like I did not because Omi
dialog: would not know what he was saying. So I did gestures that were absolutely not in
dialog: syne with what I was saying. They liked this a lot. After this test, the brief for the
dialog: character expanded a little bit. I was told that the character is a top honcho of a
text: corporate concern, always in a suit with a whole lot of expensive and fancy stuff.
dialog: He has come back to India to prove a point to these guys who used to make fun
dialog: of him. This was what I was given to play with. I was asked again not to worry
dialog: about the Hindi. If I so wanted, then I could do it all in English. So I did this
dialog: test even better. I smoked a ciggarette and did my thing. All the Hindi words that
dialog: I knew were the bad words so I sprinkled what I was saying with that. My mother
dialog: was there with me so she was a bit shocked.
dialog: I did the audition well but I was quite sure that I was not right for the role because I
dialog: did not speak the language at all. I went back to LA after this test and did not
dialog: hear from the 3 /diots team for a month. In LA, if you don’t get a call for a week
dialog: after an audition, it can be safely assumed that you didn’t get the job.
dialog: But a month later, I got a call from the production team and this time I was told
dialog: that I would be auditioning with scenes from 3 Idiots. I knew then that I was in
dialog: the race and, therefore, being seriously considered. Then I started studying the text.
dialog: They gave me the now famous balatkar speech scene. The other scene they gave
dialog: 382
dialog: me was the water tank scene where I lose my temper and the bet between Rancho
dialog: and Chatur is made. I could see that they wanted to gauge whether I could do
dialog: both comedy and drama. The Broken Horses team was in New Mexico so I flew
dialog: to New Mexico and did the audition. I gave it my best shot. I gave up a role to
dialog: make it for this audition and I don’t regret my decision. Again, I did not get a
dialog: call from the team for another month. Then Raju called me himself and told me
dialog: that though I had done a great job someone had taught me how to speak those
dialog: lines. He was right because my mom had taught me. He told me to stop learning
dialog: Hindi as this character would know very little Hindi. They were writing him like
dialog: that, therefore, if I wanted the role, I would have to stop learning Hindi and stop
dialog: watching Hindi movies. He did not want my interpretation of the character and
dialog: performance to be affected by such factors.
dialog: Things work differently here than in the West. Did you have any apprehensions
dialog: about being away from the Hollywood scene for the considerable duration
dialog: 3 Idiots demanded you to be away?
dialog: I had my doubts because I was giving up six months of my life and not being paid
dialog: much according to Hollywood standards. I worried about whether this was the right
dialog: choice or not. But the very reason I chose this as my preferred field of work was
dialog: because I wanted to be able to do new, fun, and exciting things. This would offer
dialog: all the things I looked for in a job. It was a new system and sensibility I would
dialog: be operating in. Therefore, I wanted to do this and it was my choice. Also Raju’s
dialog: sincerity and personal emails to me convinced me that I would be taken care of.
dialog: Once I came down here and saw the level of professional courtesy I was extended,
dialog: the sincerity and the seriousness, I knew I had taken the right decision.
dialog: What were your first reactions to the script?
dialog: That it is a very long one (laughs). There were lots of pages. I read through it
dialog: but I kept my focus on Chatur. I had not thought of the film as a whole. I only
dialog: focussed on my character. When I saw the full film, that is when I realised what
dialog: the whole film was all about. I really just kept my mind on Chatur. Also my lack
dialog: of knowledge of the language kept me from understanding a lot of things.
dialog: You were already dealing with language issues. I am sure there were portions
dialog: in the film that were more challenging than others. Tell us a little about those
dialog: tough ones you had to get your head around?
speaker_heading: 383
text: It has to be the scene on the water tank where I am angry and drunk. I am a
dialog: happy person and emotions like anger and jealousy do not come easily to me.
dialog: Before performing this scene, I had to separate myself from everyone to be able
dialog: to do a good job. The anger and depression had to be worked on. I had a three-
dialog: day break before we were to shoot this scene. I requested Raju to let me leave the
dialog: production for those three days and to just put me up in a small room or club
dialog: or hotel somewhere away from everyone. Raju agreed and I isolated myself. When
text: I reached the set three days later, I kept to myself and let the isolation gnaw at me.
dialog: Raju loved my work in that scene. It took me another three days after the scene
dialog: to come out of the state I had driven myself into though.
dialog: ‘What was the experience like for you since this is your first brush with Hindi
dialog: Cinema?
dialog: The whole experience was very different from America. It was very interesting.
dialog: I really wanted to do this and I am glad I did. This was primarily because I knew
dialog: that Raju wanted me and it is very rare and different from America where whether
dialog: the director wants you or does not want you does not matter because the corporate
dialog: guys make the decision. Here, the director really liked me and wanted me and
dialog: we had developed a personal connection. Systems-wise, things were completely
dialog: different from the West. Here make-up and costume trials were done in your hotel
dialog: room which was a little odd. I got the script five days before we began shooting.
dialog: Though it took some getting used to, all of this had its own flavour. Also, I am
dialog: not from here so I don’t really have hang ups about chatting and mixing around
dialog: with the drivers and kitchen staff. So I could absorb a lot and get in touch with
dialog: the ground realities a lot better as I chatted with these people. These guys have
text: the most interesting stories. The others are more politically correct and talk about
dialog: show business all the time. The biggest perk of the role was that I was playing a fat
dialog: balding guy in major chunks of the film for which I had to put on weight. I put
dialog: on twelve kilograms and had a lot of fun doing that because I could eat whatever
dialog: I wanted and did not have to exercise.
dialog: — Smriti Kiran

speaker_heading: PROFILES
dialog: 
speaker_heading: RAJKUMAR HIRANI
dialog: Rajkumar Hirani graduated from the Film and Television
dialog: Institute of India specialising in editing, He went on
dialog: to edit films, serials and ad films before starting his own company,
dialog: Canvas Films, to make ad films.
dialog: He produced and directed over 200 ad films before making his
dialog: feature film debut with the path-breaking Munna Bhai M.B.B.S.
dialog: He followed it up with Lage Raho Munna Bhai. Both these films
dialog: won numerous awards including the National Film Award. Raju’
text: third film as writer, editor and director is 3 Idiots.

speaker_heading: ABHIJAT JOSHI
dialog: Abhijat Joshi isan M.RA. from the University of Texas, Austin, and
dialog: a multilingual playwright, screenwriter and Professor of Creative
text: Writing at Otterbein College, Westerville, Ohio. His play, A Shaft
dialog: of Sunlight, which explored sectarian violence in Gujarat, won
dialog: the BBC World Service Playwriting Contest in 1993, and was
dialog: published and performed extensively in London. He won the
dialog: National Film Award in 2007 for the screenplay of Lage Raho
dialog: Munna Bhai. His most recent work as a writer is Rajkumar Hirani’s
dialog: 3 Idiots.

speaker_heading: SHANTANU MOITRA
dialog: Shantanu Moitra is the music director of landmark films
dialog: like Parineeta, Lage Raho Munna Bhai, Hazaaron Khwaishein
text: Aisi, Eklavya: The Royal Guard, 3 Idiots, and the Bangla
dialog: film, Antaheen.

text: the world. After quitting client servicing in an advertising agency,
dialog: Shantanu started composing jingles. “Bole mere lips, I love Uncle
dialog: Chips? was his first advertisement and he has scored for more than
dialog: 2000 advertisements. In 2007, he won the Cannes Bronze Lion
dialog: for scoring the Happydent advertisement.
text: Shantanu is a keen trekker, avid photographer and an amateur
dialog: astronomer, In 2006, he won the R.D. Burman Filmfare Award
dialog: for New Music Talent.

speaker_heading: VIDHU VINOD CHOPRA
dialog: Vidhu Vinod Chopra studied filmmaking at the Film &
dialog: Television Institute of India, Pune. His first documentary,
text: An Encounter With Faces, highlighted the plight of India’s destitute
dialog: children. It was nominated for an Oscar in the Documentary
dialog: Short Subject category in 1979.
dialog: Since then, he has directed films like Khamosh, Parinda, 1942: A
dialog: Love Story, Kareeb, Mission Kashmir and Eklavya: The Royal Guard.
dialog: Of these, Parinda and Eklavya: The Royal Guard were nominated
dialog: as India’s official entries to the Oscars in the Best Foreign Film
dialog: category in 1989 and 2007 respectively.
dialog: His film company — Vinod Chopra Films — has also produced
dialog: films like Munna Bhai M.B.B.S., Parineeta, Lage Raho Munna
text: Bhai and 3 Idiots. Vinod Chopra Films continues to receive
dialog: national and international recognition.

speaker_heading: PUBLISHER'S NOTE
speaker_heading: OM
speaker_heading: Om Books International
dialog: 
scene_heading: 11_Publisher's note.indd 422 5/31/2010 1:53:57 AM
text: A WorD OF THANKS
dialog: Within a span of 10 months — between August 2009 and June 2010 — Om Books
text: International has published the original screenplays of two unparalleled blockbusters of
dialog: Hindi cinema — Lage Raho Munna Bhai and now 3 Idiots — directed by Rajkumar
dialog: Hirani and produced by Vinod Chopra Films. This would not have been possible
dialog: without the trust and faith Vidhu Vinod Chopra, Anupama Chopra, Rajkumar
text: Hirani and Abhijat Joshi reposed in Om Books International. For this, not only
dialog: am I grateful to all of them, I am humbled as well.
dialog: Smriti Kiran conceptualised and compiled 3 Idiots: The Original Screenplay. 1 thank
dialog: her for her efforts.
dialog: I thank Shraboni Roy, our graphic designer, Sangita Koushik, our editor, Sanjay
dialog: Gupta and Raj John, for their support.
dialog: And last, but not least, I would like to thank Dipa Chaudhuri for not losing sight
dialog: of our common goal till the very end. Without her inputs and patience, this book
dialog: would have fallen short of the expectations of those who wrote the original screenplay
text: of 3 Idiots and turned it into a landmark film and a modern classic.
dialog: — Ajay Mago
speaker_heading: 423
scene_heading: 11_Publisher's note.indd 423 5/31/2010 1:53:57 AM
dialog: 
dialog: 
dialog: 
dialog: SMRITI KIRAN covered entertainment, particularly the Hindi film industry for over 11 years as a journalist and
text: television producer at NDTV, before joining the industry at Vinod Chopra Films where she worked on a film
text: under development, and briefly on 3 Idiots.
text: Her Twitter profile (smritikiran) describes her as a cinema junkie, freelance television producer and journalist,
text: writer, aspiring filmmaker, voracious reader and coffee addict. Her husband agrees with the coffee addict part.
text: In between reading primarily Indian authors and poring over Hindi film scripts, she works on her own script
dialog: which she hopes to direct into a film one dai
dialog: 
text: Apart from following her dreams and continuing to learn from the best in the business, she leads the screenplays
text: division at Vinod Chopra Films, a venture which aims to bring screenplays of the greatest Indian classics and
text: more recent films which contribute to the dynamism of Indian cinema, to cinema buffs and readers across
dialog: the world.
dialog: smriti.kiran@gmail.com
